Norell, Clark and Makovicky, 2001. Phylogenetic relationships among coelurosaurian theropods. In Gauthier and Gall (eds.). New Perspectives on the Origin and Early Evolution of Birds: Proceedings of the International Symposium in Honor of John H. Ostrom. 49-67.

This is the first published matrix by the Theropod Working Group, which has gone on to be modified in over forty publications, making it the most popular dataset for coelurosaur phylogeny.

Phylogeny

Norell et al. reported and illustrated this consensus of 1065 mpts of 554 steps each.

However, running their matrix results in 170100 mpts of 549 steps each, as shown below. The difference is that Dromaeosauridae is monophyletic with Sinornithosaurus and Unenlagia basal members, and Sinornithoides is less derived than Byronosaurus.

Taxon issues

Allosaurus fragilis- A. "jimmadseni" is also used for coding.
Albertosaurus libratus- This is referred to Gorgosaurus in most recent studies.
Struthiomimus altus- The unnamed Horseshoe Canyon species is also used for coding, as it was not recognized in 2001.
Shuvuuia deserti- IGM 100/99 was referred to this species at this time, but may be more closely related to Parvicursor. It is not used in coding.
IGM 100/1015- This has been subsequently named Tsaagan mangas.
Sinornithosaurus milleni- NGMC 91 is not used for coding. The species name has since been corrected to S. millenii.
Unenlagia comahuensis- While many later versions of this matrix combine the possibly synonymous Neuquenraptor with Unenlagia into one OTU, only the Unenlagia comahuensis holotype is used for coding here.
Saurornithoides junior- This has since been renamed Zanabazar.
Chirostenotes pergracilis- This uses the Horseshoe Canyon specimen described by Sues (1997) for coding, but not the Hell Creek specimens. It also uses Caenagnathus collinsi, which Norell et al. appear to code from.
Caudipteryx zoui- C. dongi and C. sp. of Zhou et al. (2000) are used for coding as well.
Oviraptor mongoliensis- This is referred to its own genus Rinchenia by recent workers.
IGM 100/42- While many more recent versions of this matrix have conflated/combined its codings with Citipati osmolskae, only IGM 100/42 is used here.
Ingenia yanshini- The name Ingenia is preoccupied by a nematode, so "Ingenia" is used here.

Coding issues

1. This character (symmetry of remiges) is uninformative, since only three taxa can be coded.

2. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) have a naris entirely anterior to the antorbital fossa. IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo) and "Ingenia" (coding in Lu, 2004) have a naris which extends posteriorly to overlap the antorbital fossa. Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) is polymorphic. The condition in Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) is unknown.

3. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2008) lack extensive dorsal nasal pneumatization. Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002) cannot be coded.

4. Pelecanimimus lacks a maxillary fenestra, as its single fenestra is low (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994) and thus matches ornithomimosaur promaxillary fenestrae. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a maxillary fenestra. The condition in oviraptorids is confusing due to their highly modified and pneumatic skulls. Here, I identify the large anteriorly located fenestra as promaxillary, while a more posterodorsally placed and small fenestra is interpreted as the maxillary. Rinchenia (unpublished photo) has a maxillary fenestra and Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999) lacks one. While Oviraptor has an opening in the position of the maxillary fenestra, this may be damage as many other small artificial openings are present as well. IGM 100/42 is polymorphic, with an opening on the left side but not the right (unpublished photos).

5. "Ingenia" (coding in Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) lack a distinct rim on the antorbital fossa. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1996), Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Struthiomimus (Rauhut, 2003), Gallimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999), Sinornithoides (Xu and Wu, 2001) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) have a distinct rim. Shuvuuia does not preserve a medial antorbital fossa wall, so cannot be coded for the transition between that wall and the external surface. Troodon's coding was changed by Senter (2007), but as the condition is not determinable in described specimens, it is left unknown here pending further data.

6. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Struthiomimus (Sereno, 2001; Russell, 1972), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) and Troodon (Currie, 1985) have an anteriorly placed maxillary fenestra. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a posteriorly placed maxillary fenestra. The condition in Shuvuuia (as the presence of a maxillary fenestra is unknown), Oviraptor (presence of maxillary fenestra uncertain) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) are unknown. Pelecanimimus is inapplicable.

7.Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (maxillary fenestra of Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Troodon (Currie, 1985), and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack a promaxillary fenestra. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Witmer, 1997), Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Oviraptor (pers. obs.), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) and Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) have a promaxillary fenestra.

8. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 2005), Troodon (Currie, 1985), Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1978) and Adasaurus (unpublished photo) have round orbits. Archaeopteryx (Erickson et al., 2009) cannot be coded as all specimens with preserved orbits are juveniles, which have round orbits even in tyrannosaurids.

9. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) has an orbital process on the postorbital. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Troodon (Currie, 1985) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) lack a process. The ventral process of Avimimus' postorbital is incomplete (Kurzanov, 1985), so cannot be coded. Archaeopteryx only preserves this in the Berlin specimen, which is a juvenile, so cannot be coded. Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) does not preserve the postorbital.

10. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) has a postorbital bar which is highly convex posteriorly. Gorgosaurus is polymorphic, as AMNH 5434 (Carr, 2005) has a strongly convex posterior margin. As Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001) and Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1986) lack a dorsal jugal process, the amount the postorbital bar constricts the laterotemporal fenestra cannot be measured. Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) does not preserve the postorbital.

11. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) have a suborbital fenestra larger than 25% of orbital height. Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002) doesn't preserve an ectopterygoid, so the presence of a suborbital fenestra is uncertain.

12. The enlarged pneumatic recess referred to here is the 'lateral depression' first noted by Barsbold (1974) in Zanabazar. Zanabazar's lateral depression is formed by the anterior tympanic recess, subotic recess and otic recess, which are surrounded by the prominent otosphenoidal crest anteriorly, the paroccipital process posterodorsally, metotic strut posteriorly, and a lateral ridge across the basal tuber posteroventrally. Troodon has less association between the otic recess and lateral depression, while they are separated by a wide surface in Saurornithoides and Byronosaurus. Chirostenotes was described as having a similar structure to troodontids', but lacks a subotic recess and notable posterior or posteroventral wall, as the otic recess and anterior tympanic recess are both visible posteriorly (though the basal tuber itself provides a slight wall) (Sues, 1997). Thus while Chirostenotes has a well developed otosphenoidal crest (though slightly sigmoid as opposed to crescent-shaped), it does not border a recess any more extensive than the anterior tympanic recess. Of other taxa Norell et al. code as having this character, Archaeopteryx has a low horizontal otosphenoidal crest separating the anterior tympanic recess from the otic recess, seemingly homologous to a partition within the lateral depression of Troodon. The Munich specimen lacks obvious anterior or posteroventral walls and has a subotic recess widely separated from the anterior tympanic recess (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996). Finally, Shuvuuia has low, acutely pointed otosphenioidal crest and lacks any ridge across the basal tuber which could join the anterior tympanic recess and otic recess in a lateral depression. It lacks a subotic recess (Dufeau, 2003). Thus only troodontids can be said to have an enlarged pneumatic recess / lateral depression, which is here defined explicitly by the placement of the anterior tympanic recess and subotic recess into a common depression. This definition requires scoring some taxa (including Gorgosaurus, Tyrannosaurus, Shuvuuia, Mononykus, Chirostenotes, Tsaagan, Velociraptor and Dromaeosaurus) inapplicable, since they lack a subotic recess (character 17) which could help form the lateral depression. Note too that NEA01's character description is problematic in that the plesiomorphic condition as seen in carnosaurs and tyrannosauroids is to have a horizontal crest, not a vertical one, and that the large overhanging crest in these taxa is more well developed than any taxon supposed to have a lateral depression with the possible exception of Chirostenotes. Since otosphenoidal crest size, shape and orientation vary independently, they are best not used in a character coding a lateral depression, though they may make useful additional characters. Erlikosaurus (Perle, 1981) has a lateral depression. The condition cannot be determined in IGM 100/42 due to tight adherence of the quadrate and pterygoid (unpublished photos).

13. Gorgosaurus (Bakker et al., 1988), Struthiomimus (Makovicky and Norell, 1998), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1986) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) have large paired foramina in their basisphenoid recess. Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) can be marked inapplicable for lacking the basisphenoid recess.

14. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2000) have a depressed interfenestral crest.

15. Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janacek, 2007) lack paracondylar recesses. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Makiovicky and Norell, 1998), Troodon (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) and Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) have paracondylar recesses.

16. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Gorgosaurus (Carr, 2005), Shuvuuia (unpublished photo), Sinornithoides (Bever and Norell, 2009), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Saurornithoides (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Archaeopteryx (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) lack basipterygoid recesses. Allosaurus (Chure and Madsen, 1996) is polymorphic. Erlikosaurus, Rinchenia, IGM 100/42 and Conchoraptor lack basipterygoid processes, so should be coded inapplicable. Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002) does not preserve the basipterygoid area, so cannot be coded.

17. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janecek, 2007) and Sinornithoides (Makovicky and Norell, 2004) have a subotic recess. Shuvuuia (unpublished photo), Mononykus (coding in Hwang et al., 2004) and Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) lack a subotic recess. Allosaurus is polymorphic (Chure and Madsen, 1996- 'caudoventral recess').

18. The basisphenoid recess of Tyrannosaurus (Bakker et al., 1988) and Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) contacts the basioccipital. The basisphenoid recess of Rinchenia (coding in Lu, 2004) lies entirely within the basisphenoid. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) has a recess of unknown position, so is coded 0/1. Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) lack a basisphenoid recess. Chirostenotes also lacks a basisphenoid recess (Sues, 1997), though it retains the paired recesses which are within the basisphenoid recess of many other coelurosaurs.

19. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999) lack an expanded parasphenoid. Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004) and Sinornithoides (Makovicky and Norell, 2004) have an inflated parasphenoid.

20. Gorgosaurus (Bakker et al., 1988) and Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1986) has vertically oriented basipterygoid processes. Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) and Velociraptor (Norell et al., 2004) have laterally oriented (>50 degrees from vertical) basipterygoid processes. Rinchenia, IGM 100/42 and Conchoraptor lack basipterygoid processes, so should be coded inapplicable. Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002) does not preserve the basipterygoid area.

21. Gorgosaurus (Bakker et al., 1988) has large basipterygoid processes. Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Rinchenia (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002) and Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002) have reduced basipterygoid processes. Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002) does not preserve the basipterygoid area.

22. Sinornithoides (Bever and Norell, 2009) has pneumatized basipterygoid processes. Rinchenia and Conchoraptor lack basipterygoid processes, so should be coded inapplicable.

23. This character (dorsal tympanic recess presence and depth) should be ordered, since a shallow recess is intermediate between an absent and a deep process. Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) lacks a dorsal tympanic recess. Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Oviraptor (Clarke et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984) and Dromaeosaurus (Turner et al., 2011) have a shallow recess. Conchoraptor is polymorphic, with both a shallow (coding in Hwang et al., 2004) and deep recess (Kundrat and Janecek, 2007). Deinonychus is coded with an uncertainty polymorphy, as it has a recess of unknown depth (Brinkman et al., 1998).

24. This character should be ordered for the same reason 23 should. Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) lacks an accessory recess above the fenestra ovalis. Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) has a small pocket dorsal to the crista interfenestralis. Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002- based on pneumatic parietals), Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janacek, 2007) and "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984; Osmolska, 2004) have an extensively developed recess.

25. This character should also be ordered, as both states 1 and 2 share the presence of a posterior tympanic recess. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 2005), Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janacek, 2007) and "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984) have a posterior tympanic recess opening on the paroccipital process. Pelecanimimus (Senter, 2007), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Troodon (Currie and Zhao, 1994) have their opening confluent with the otic recess. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Rinchenia (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Norell et al., 2006) can be scored 1/2 based on the hollow paroccipital process which indicate the presence of a posterior tympanic recess.

26. This character should be ordered because state 0 (premaxillary subnarial process contacts nasal but does not extend posterior to naris) is intermediate between states 1 (subnarial process does not contact nasal) and 2 (subnarial process contacts nasal and extends posterior to naris). However, this necessitates switching states 0 and 1. Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) have a short subnarial process that contacts the nasal. IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo) has a subnarial process extending posterior to the naris. Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Saurornitholestes (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004), Deinonychus (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) and Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) have a subnarial process which at least contacts the nasal, though its posterior extent in unknown.

27. This character (posterior premaxillary process short and blunt (0) or elongate and extend along nasal-maxillary suture posterior to nares (1)) is the same as state 2 of character 26, though oddly Ornitholestes and Archaeopteryx are also coded as having it. It is deleted and all 25 scored taxa rescored as ?.

28. Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Oviraptor (Osborn, 1924; assuming the vaulted frontals are part of a crest connecting to the premaxilla which is high anterior to the naris) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) has a round internarial bar. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999; oddly coded inapplicable by Norell et al.) have a flattened internarial bar.

29. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) and Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) have a V-shaped premaxillary symphysis. Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976), Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1999), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) and Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) have a U-shaped premaxillary symphysis.

30. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001; Chiappe et al., 2002) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969; Paul, unpublished) do not preserve the ventral premaxillary margin, so cannot be coded for crenulation.

31. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002), Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Troodon (Currie and Dong, 2001), Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004), Deinonychus (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004), Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) and Archaeopteryx (based on maxillary articular facets on vomer in Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) have extensive maxillary secondary palates.

32. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2003), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) and Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) lack a ventral tooth-like maxillary process. "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) has a ventral tooth-like process on the maxilla. The area in Oviraptor is unpreserved (Clark et al., 2002).

33. There are two ways of evaluating this character (Jugal and postorbital contribute equally to postorbital bar (0), or ascending process of jugal reduced and descending process of postorbital ventrally elongate (1)). One could measure the length of the dorsal jugal process or of the ventral postorbital process, which are independent since they usually overlap. Using the dorsal jugal process results in better agreement with Norell et al.'s matrix (where only ornithomimids are coded as apomorphic), so that is followed here. Rinchenia (Barsbold et al., 1990), Conchoraptor (Barsbold, 1986), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) have a tall dorsal postorbital process. Pelecanimimus (Makovicky et al., 2004), Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002; coded inapplicable by Norell et al., but an absent dorsal process is a form of 'reduced process'), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have short dorsal processes.

34. Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) has a strap-like posterior jugal process. Struthiomimus was coded as inapplicable for the transverse width of the posterior jugal process compared to its depth. Yet Struthiomimus has a posterior jugal process, just one that is overlapped by the quadratojugal (Osborn, 1916; Knutsen, 2007). It is thus recoded "?". Zanabazar's posterior process is missing (Norell et al., 2009), so cannot be coded.

35. Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) and Deinonychus (Witmer, 1997) have a pneumatic jugal foramen in the antorbital fossa. Garudimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) and Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) lack an anterior jugal foramen.

36. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) lacks a medial jugal foramen. Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) has one. Avimimus is coded as inapplicable, but with a jugal comparable to Shuvuuia which is coded as lacking the foramen, this seems uneccessary.

37. This character (T-shaped quadratojugal) is most congruent with Norell et al.'s scoring when it is defined as a posterior quadratojugal process over 35% of the bone's length anterior to it. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Chure, 2001), Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Avimimus (Kuzanov, 1985), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) and Archaeopteryx (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) have L-shaped quadratojugals. Saurornitholoestes (Currie, 1995) has a T-shaped quadratojugal. Caudipteryx is polymorphic, with some specimens (Zhou et al., 2000) having L-shaped quadratojugals.

38. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Oviraptor (unpublished photos), "Ingenia" (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have an unfused jugal and quadratojugal.

39. Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Troodon (Currie, 1985) lack a dorsal or lateral lacrimal horn. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994) has a dorsal horn. Struthiomimus (Knutsen, 2007), Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000; left lacrimal misidentified as part of nasal), Conchoraptor (Barsbold et al., 1990), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have a lateral expansion. IGM 100/42 is polymorphic, with a lateral expansion on the left side but not the right side (unpublished photos). The condition in Erlikosaurus is uncertain due to medial displacement of the lacrimal (Clark et al., 1994), so can only be coded 0/2. As Senter (2007) coded Adasaurus 0, but miscoded other dromaeosaurids with that state too, it is here conservatively coded 0/2. The supposed lacrimal of Utahraptor is a Gastonia postorbital (Britt et al., 2001).

40. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (coding in Senter, 2003) lack a lacrimal foramen. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Oviraptor (Smith, 1992), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) and Deinonychus (Witmer, 1997) have a lacrimal foramen. The supposed lacrimal of Utahraptor is a Gastonia postorbital (Britt et al., 2001).

41. As Senter (2007) notes, the state "Lacrimal anterodorsal process absent (inverted L-shaped)" was probably supposed to indicate the posterodorsal process, as among the included taxa only Confuciusornis lacks an anterodorsal process. However, this makes the character a composite, since state 2 (anterodorsal process much longer than posterior process) involves anterodorsal process size and both processes can vary independently. It's also problematic because the anterodorsal process is much longer than the posterodorsal process in taxa with L-shaped lacrimals, since they're L-shaped due to having a very small posterodorsal process. What taxa with state 2 (troodontids) are actually characterized by is a posterodorsal process which is long compared to the ventral process (>130%). So this character is redefined as- anterodorsal and posterodorsal process short (0); anterodorsal process >130% of ventral process length (1); posterodorsal process >30% of ventral process length (2), unordered and with the acknowledgement taxa can have both 1 and 2 simultaneously. It should also be unordered, as having state 2 doesn't entail having state 1. Pelecanimimus (Makovicky et al., 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) have both processes short. Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Oviraptor (Clark et al., 2002), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), IGM 100/42 (Barsbold et al., 1990), Conchoraptor (Osmolska, 1976), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a long posterodorsal process. Some Tyrannosaurus (Carr and Williamson, 2004) have elongate posterodorsal processes, so it is recoded 0+1. Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003; Bever and Norell, 2009) have both processes elongate, though note the juveniles lack the elongate anterior process. Dromaeosaurus has a long posterodorsal process (based on the frontal's suture; Currie, 1995) at least, so can be coded 1/2. Similarly, Avimimus' posterodorsal process is long (Kurzanov, 1981), though the anterodorsal process length is uncertain. Saurornithoides' lacrimal is too worn to determine if either process were long (Norell et al., 2009). The supposed lacrimal of Utahraptor is a Gastonia postorbital (Britt et al., 2001).

42. This should be ordered, as an absent prefrontal is an extreme form of a reduced prefrontal. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.) and Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) have unreduced prefrontals. Shuvuuia is here interpreted as having unreduced prefrontals (Sereno, 2001), as the basal alvarezsauroid Haplocheirus does as well and no ectethmoids are known to be dorsally exposed or present in non-avian theropods. Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) have a reduced prefrontal. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1981), Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) lack a prefrontal.

43. For this character, a 'transverse' nasofrontal suture is defined as one whose angle (using both sides) is <60 degrees, as this matches Norell et al.'s codings best. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004) and Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos) have a wedge-shaped nasofrontal suture. Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984), Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1978) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have a transverse nasofrontal suture. Oviraptor (unpublished photo) does not preserve the nasofrontal suture. Confuciusornis should be coded inapllicable for lacking nasofrontal contact (Chiappe et al., 1999).

44. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) lack a sinusoidal supratemporal fossa. Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1912) and Troodon (Currie, 1985) are polymorphic, as some individuals have a sinusoidal supratemporal fossa. Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001) and Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009; possibly ontogenetic) lack a frontal supratemporal fossa, so should be coded inapplicable.

45. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) have a smooth transition between the frontal's orbital margin and postorbital process. Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have a sharp transition. Allosaurus (McClelland, 1990; Chure, 2000) can have a significant lateral margin on its frontal, with a short orbital edge at a sharp angle to the longer postorbital process edge. Adult Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) lack frontal-orbital contact, so should be coded inapplicable.

46. Pelecanimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) lack a slot on the frontal's nasal-lacrimal articular edge. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (McClelland, 1990), Gorgosaurus (Currie, 1987), Tyrannosaurus (Molnar, 1991), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) have a slot on the frontal's nasal-lacrimal articular edge. Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos) and Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) cannot be coded for this character, as the anterior edge of the frontal's nasal-lacrimal articular surface is hidden under the nasal and lacrimal. The fact so many taxa are coded incorrectly or impossibly suggests that this character may have been misunderstood by Norell et al. (and Kobayashi) as something visible externally, which would leave Caudipteryx, Byronosaurus and Troodon as the only taxa confirmed as lacking the notch.

47. Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Troodon (Currie, 1985), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack a dorsally curved anterior postorbital process. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Oviraptor (Osborn, 1924), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) have a dorsally curved anterior postorbital process. Tyrannosaurus (Carr and Williamson, 2004) is polymorphic.

48. Contra Norell et al., there does not appear to be an obvious difference in height between the parietal sagittal crests of tyrannosaurids and troodontids and those of other taxa. Thus state 2 (dorsally convex with well-developed sagittal crest) is combined into state 1, which now covers all taxa with sagittal crests. Pelecanimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack a sagittal crest. Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Osmolska, 2004), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1978; based on posterior frontal), Adasaurus (Kubota and Barsbold, 2006) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have sagittal crests.

49. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have unfused parietals. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Osmolska, 2004) have fused parietals.

50. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1985; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Oviraptor (Osborn, 1924), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999), Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995), Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a ventrally oriented ventral squamosal process. Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001) should be coded inapplicable as it lacks a ventral squamosal process.

51. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) and Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) lack a squamosal shelf over the ventral process. Troodon (Currie, 1985) and Saurornitholestes (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004) have a shelf. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) is polymorphic, as the right squamosal has a shelf but the left does not. Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001) should be coded inapplicable as it lacks a ventral squamosal process. Zanabazar's condition is unknown, as the proximolateral surface is missing (Norell et al., 2009). Archaeopteryx should be coded unknown, as the only preserved squamosal (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) is in medial view.

52. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Avimimus (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Oviraptor (Osborn, 1924), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004), Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) have squamosal-quadratojugal contact. Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Archaeopteryx (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack contact. Garudimimus cannot be coded, as the tips of each bone are missing (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005).

53. This character is written as "dorsal process of quadrate single-headed (0) or with two distinct heads, a lateral one contacting the squamosal and a medial head contacting the prootic (1)". However, note that taxa can have two indistinct heads which contact the squamosal and braincase (e.g. oviraptorids) and still be coded 0. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1996), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Deinonychus (Burnham, 2004) lack a distinct otic head on the quadrate.

54. This character is here defined as having a quadrate inclined posterodorsally >105 degrees compared to the ventral quadratojugal-jugal edge, as this matches best with Norell et al.'s codings. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have a non-inclined quadrate. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) have an inclined quadrate.

55. This character involves the presence of a "lateral tab that touches squamosal and quadratojugal above an enlarged quadrate foramen" on the quadrate. It is basically caused by the transverse width of the paraquadrate foramen, and is here measured as the width from the paraquadrate foramen's medial edge to the lateral edge of the dorsal quadrate, compared to the width of the quadrate condyles. Using this measure, Sinraptor has a wide 'tab' due not to the paraquadrate foramen's size, but to its medial position within the quadrate. As Sinraptor does not technically have a tab due to the paraquadrate foramen being surrounded instead of an incision, it is here scored inapplicable. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1985), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (Maryanska and Osmolska, 1997), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) have narrow tabs. Tyrannosaurus (Larson, 2008), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Deinonychus (Paul, unpublished) have wide tabs. Note the different codings from Norell et al. mean that Gorgosaurus may be found to have a wide tab, and Rinchenia and IGM 100/42 narrow tabs, once they are measured.

56. While this character is stated to code for the presence of a quadrate which is "hollow, with depression on posterior surface", I merely code for pneumaticity regardless of the presence of a posterior fossa. Caudipteryx (coding in Xu et al., 1999), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004), Saurornitholestes (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Deinonychus (coding in Xu et al., 2004) and Confuciusornis (Clarke, 2002) have solid quadrates. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janecek, 2007), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) have a pneumatic quadrate.

57. Garudimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Struthiomimus (Makovicky and Norell, 1998), Conchoraptor (Maryanska and Osmolska, 2007) and "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) have a foramen magnum wider than tall. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1985), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) and Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) have a tall foramen magnum. Allosaurus is polymorphic (Chure, 2000).

58. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Struthiomimus (Makovicky and Norell, 1998), "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984), Deinonychus (coding in Clark et al., 2002) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) lack an occipital condyle neck. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Conchoraptor (Kundrat and Janacek, 2007) have a transversely constricted occipital condyle base. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) and Troodon (Currie, 1985) are polymorphic, with some specimens lacking a constricted neck.

59. This character codes for both paroccipital process elongation and distal tapering, but as distinct dorsal and ventral edges are often curved or hard to define on the structure only elongation is coded here. The derived state is defined as a midlength height >70% of the length from the occipital condyle edge to the tip. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Troodon (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Sinornithosaurus (Turner et al., 2011)***, Adasaurus (Turner et al., 2011) and Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) have long processes. Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), IGM 100/42 (as coded by Senter, 2007; provisionally accepted due to the coding in other oviraptorids), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) have short processes.

60. Saurornithoides (pers. obs.), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter et al., 2004) have laterally directed paroccipital processes. Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) and Archaeopteryx (Alonso et al., 2004) have ventrally angled paroccipital processes, specified here as having a central axis more than 40 degrees below horizontal.

61. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Adasaurus (Kubota and Barsbold, 2006) lack twisted paroccipital processes. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1996), Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) have twisted paroccipital processes. Tyrannosaurus is polymorphic (Larson, 2008).

62. Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Zanabazar (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Troodon (Rauhut, 2003) have a ventral ectopterygoid fossa. Norell et al. (2009) note that a ventral fossa cannot be identified in Saurornithoides. Archaeopteryx's ectopterygoids are only preserved in dorsal view (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996; Paul, 2002; Mayr et al., 2007), so cannot be coded for the presence of a ventral fossa.

63. Tyrannosaurus (Larson, 2008), Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999) and Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) lack a dorsal ectopterygoid fossa. Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a dorsal fossa. Shuvuuia should be coded inapplicable for this character if it was for the last, as Norell et al. are assuming it lacks an ectopterygoid.

64. Note this character refers to the size of the low anterolateral pterygoid flange medial to and in front of the ectopterygoid, not the triangular flange bracing the latter from behind. While oviraptorids have a highly modified palate, CT data of Citipati (Digimorph) show that the pterygoid has a lateral flange, albeit one enclosed laterally by the ectopterygoid and palatine. The same configuration seems to be present in IGM 100/42 (Barsbold et al., 1990) and Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999; though the palatine-pterygoid suture is incorrectly located). Due to this, Oviraptor's and Rinchenia's contrary codings by Norell et al. and Lu (2004) respectively are omitted until their palates are better known. Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a large flange.

65. Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) have a tetraradiate palatine. Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999) have a triradiate palatine.

66. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1996), Ornitholestes (pers. obs.) and Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009; due to the absence of a pterygoid process on its palatine) lack palatine-ectopterygoid contact. Conchoraptor (Elzanowski, 1999), Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) have palatine-ectopterygoid contact. Shuvuuia should be coded inapplicable for this character if it was for 62, as Norell et al. are assuming it lacks an ectopterygoid.

67. This character should be ordered, as slight recurvature is intermediate between no and strong recurvature. Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lacks a medially recurved dentary symphysis. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Struthiomimus (Knutsen, 2007) and Microvenator (pers. obs.) have a slightly medially recurved dentary symphysis. Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010) has a strongly recurved symphysis. Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001; Chiappe et al., 2002) does not preserve the anterior dentary, so cannot be coded.

68. Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Caudipteryx (Ji et al., 1998; Zhou et al., 2000) and Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) lack a large coronoid process on the surangular. Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) has a large process. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) do not preserve a surangular.

69. This character was originally designed by Currie to separate the roughly parallel dorsal and ventral dentary edges of dromaeosaurids from the posteriorly diverging edges of other theropods. It is here quantified as an anterior depth at the symphysis 'chin' <60% of greatest posterior depth being a triangular dentary, as this corresponds best with Norell et al.'s codings. All oviraptorosaurs (except Microvenator, which is coded unknown) are coded an inapplicable, but Microvenator (pers. obs.), Chirostenotes (Currie et al. 1993), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), Rinchenia (Barsbold et al., 1990), IGM 100/42 (Barsbold et al., 1990), Conchoraptor (Osmolska, 1976) and "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 1990) have diverging edges, while Caudipteryx (Ji et al., 1998; Zhou et al., 2000) has subparallel edges. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979), Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have subparallel dentary margins. Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1912) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) have a triangular dentary. Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) does not preserve the ventral dentary margin, so cannot be coded.

70. Shuvuuia (Sereno, 2001), Chirostenotes (Currie et al., 1993), Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) and Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) do not have decurved dentaries. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) has a decurved dentary.

71. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) lack a lateral dentary shelf.

72. This character should be ordered, since a short posterodorsal dentary process is intermediate between no process and a long one. Troodon (Currie, 1985) and Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) lack a process. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (unpublished photo of MOR 693), Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003), Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1912), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Hurum, 2001), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 1990) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) have posterodorsal dentary processes extending over less than half of their mandibular fenestra. Caudipteryx preserves both short (Ji et al., 1998) and long (Zhou et al., 2000) processes, so is coded 1+2. While Microvenator preserves part of a process (Makovicky and Sues, 1998), it is not known what percentage of the mandibular fenestra it covers. It is recoded 1/2. Archaeopteryx does not preserve the posterior dentary (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996; Paul, 2002; Tischlinger, 2005, 2009) so cannot be coded.

73. Norell et al. used the deep lateral dentary groove to separate troodontids from other taxa, but the character is complicated by the fact lateral grooves are common in theropods and groove depth is not easily compared (especially via photographs and illustrations). However, at least Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) appear to have grooves as deep as troodontids', and Gorgosaurus (Currie, 2003) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) may as well. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Maryanska, 1990), Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) and Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) lack a deep groove. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) cannot be coded as only the dentary tip is known, which lacks the groove in e.g. Zanabazar.

74. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) lack a surangular prong bifurcating its external mandibular fenestra. Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) has a surangular prong. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000) is polymorphic, with some specimens having a surangular prong. Archaeopteryx should be coded inapplicable (Tischlinger, 2005), as it has no external mandibular fenestra to be bifurcated by a surangular prong.

75. This is specified to require a prearticular with an anterior concavity, as Turner et al. (2007) later add a character for a posterior splenial concavity. Gorgosaurus (Carr, 1996) has a slit-like internal mandibular fenestra. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Saurornitholestes (Currie, 1995) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have rounded internal mandibular fenestrae. Tyrannosaurus is polymorphic, with some specimens (Brochu, 2003) lacking a round fenestra.

76. Ornitholestes (coding in Senter, 2007), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) lack a posterior surangular foramen. Gallimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1993), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Adasaurus (Kubota and Barsbold, 2006) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) have a posterior surangular foramen.

77. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000) lack significant lateral splenial exposure. Saurornitholestes (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004) has significant exposure.

78. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) and Ornitholestes (Brochu, 2003) have a coronoid, though of unreported size. Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) has a small coronoid. Segnosaurus (coding in Clark et al., 2004), Chirostenotes (Currie et al., 1993), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), IGM 100/42 (Barsbold, 1984), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) lack a coronoid.

79. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) lack a dorsomedial articular process. Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Saurornitholestes (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004), and Velociraptor (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999) have the process. In Oviraptor the dorsal articular area is missing (unpublished photos), so it cannot be coded. Whether the elongate medial process of Shuvuuia is actually homologous to the primarily dorsal processes of dromaeosaurids and the other taxa coded as apomorphic is unknown, but it is provisionally retained as such here.

80. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Saurornitholestes (coding in Senter, 2007) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) lack an elongate retroarticular process. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Barsbold and Perle, 1984), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916) and Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002) have an elongate retroarticular process.

81. Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Sinornithosaurus (Wu and Wu, 2001) lack an elongate, convex sliding mandibular glenoid. Chirostenotes (Currie et al., 1993) and Oviraptor (unpublished photos) have an elongate, convex sliding mandibular glenoid.

82. Troodon (Currie, 1987) and Saurornitholestes (Currie et al., 1990) have premaxillary teeth. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) lacks premaxillary teeth. Oviraptor (unpublished photos) cannot be coded for the presence of premaxillary teeth, as it does not preserve the ventral premaxillary edge.

83. Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) and Dromaeosaurus (Currie, 1995) don't have a second premaxillary tooth much larger than their third and fourth. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Saurornitholestes (coding in Currie and Varricchio, 2004) and Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) have a larger second tooth. Harpymimus and "Ingenia" should be inapplicable, as they lack premaxillary teeth. Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002) and Oviraptor should be coded unknown, since the presence of premaxillary teeth is uncertain.

84. Saurornitholestes (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) has maxillary teeth. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Avimimus (Tsuhiji et al., 2008) lack maxillary teeth.

85. There are no described teeth of Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969; Brinkman et al., 1998) that naturally lack mesial serrations, so it recoded as all maxillary and dentary teeth fully serrated. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Adasaurus (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) preserve both mesial and distal serrations on at least some teeth but don't preserve all tooth positions, so should be coded 0/1. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since it lacks teeth.

86. While this character is a composite involving both maxillary/dentary tooth number and size, only the number is used here for coding. It should be ordered, since state 1 is intermediate (in number and size) between states 0 and 2. Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Maryanska, 1990) and Adasaurus (Currie and Varricchio, 2004) have less than twenty-five dentary teeth. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Troodon (Currie, 1987) have more than thirty dentary teeth. The number of dentary teeth is unknown in Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), as only twelve positions are exposed. Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) does not preserve dentary teeth. Microvenator should be inapplicable, since it lacks dentary teeth.

87. Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) has small serrations. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since it lacks teeth.

88. While this character's apomorphic state is stated as "distal and often mesial edges of teeth with large, hooked denticles that point toward the tip of the crown", serration size is here ignored as it is covered by the previous character. It should be noted that the presence of apically hooked serrations is known to show positional/individual variation in at least some dromaeosaurids, which has not been examined in most taxa. Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) and Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) lack apically hooked serrations. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994 and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) have hooked serrations. Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Saurornitholestes (Currie et al., 1990; Sankey et al., 2002) and Deinonychus (Hwang, 2011) can have apically hooked serrations, so are coded as polymorphic. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since it lacks teeth.

89. Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) lacks constricted tooth roots. Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 1994) and Archaeopteryx (Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996) are polymorphic, with only some teeth having constricted roots. Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) does not preserve maxillary or dentary teeth. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since it lacks teeth.

90. This is a composite character involving heterodonty in dentary teeth in both size and density, so only anterior tooth size compared to posterior tooth size is used here. Saurornitholestes (Sues, 1977) and Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) lack smaller anterior teeth. Harpymimus was coded inapplicable by Norell et al., but has posterior dentary teeth subequal in size to anterior ones (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005). Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998) and Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since they lack teeth.

91. Allosaurus (Masen, 1976), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Byronosaurus (Bever and Norell, 2009), Saurornithoides (Hwang and Norell, 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Martin and Stewart, 1999) lack distinct interdental plates. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.) has distinct interdental plates. Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001) is polymorphic, having unfused plates anteriorly and fused plates posteriorly. Microvenator and Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since they lack dentary teeth.

92. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Avimimus (coding in Rauhut, 2003) and Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) have joined hyposphenes. Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) have separated dorsal hyposphenes. Microvenator (pers. obs.) is polymorphic.

93. Sinraptor (coding in Benson et al., 2010), Allosaurus (McClelland, 1990), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Troodon (Currie et al., 1990), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wu, 2001), Saurornitholestes (Currie et al., 1990), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Velociraptor (Sues, 1977) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) have premaxillary teeth with flat lingual surfaces. Chirostenotes and Oviraptor should be coded unknown, since the presence of premaxillary teeth is uncertain. Harpymimus and Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable since they lack premaxillary teeth.

94. The states are defined in this character to indicate 10 (0) or 12 or more (1) cervical vertebrae, which excludes taxa with less than 10 or 11 cervicals. The character is here redefined as 10 or less (0) versus more than 10 (1). Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) have ten or less cervicals. Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002), Caudipteryx (Paul, 2002), Avimimus (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) have more than ten cervicals.

95.Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1974) and Confuciusornis (coding in Gao et al., 2008) have axial epipophyses which do not extend past their postzygapophyses. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005- based on more posterior cervicals), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004; based on postaxial cervical), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999- based on postaxial cervical) have axial epipophyses extending past their postzygapophyses.

96. "Ingenia" (coding in Lu, 2004) has a transversely flared axial neural spine. Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Rinchenia (coding in Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (coding in Lu, 2004) and Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) have a transversely compressed spine.

97. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (unpublished photo), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004), Troodon (Makovicky et al., 2004), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Velociraptor (pers. obs. of IGM 100/976) and Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1974) have cervical epipophyses placed over their postzygapophyseal facets. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Alvarezsaurus (Novas, 1996), Mononykus (Novas, 1996), Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1994) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) have cervical epipophyses placed proximal to their postzygapophyseal facets.

98. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) have anterior cervical centra not extending past their neural arch. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Avimimus (Makovicky, 1995), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Confuciusornis (coding in Senter, 2007) have anterior cervical centra extending posteriorly past the neural arch.

99. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Alvarezsaurus (Novas, 1996), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) and Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) lack carotid processes. "Ingenia" (coding in Lu, 2004) has carotid processes. Ornitholestes should be coded unknown, as only the ninth and tenth cervicals always have carotid processes in taxa with these structures, but those vertebrae are unpreserved.

100. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Microvenator (pers. obs.), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Norell et al., 2000) and Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004) have wide anterior cervical centra.

101. This character (cervical neural spines anteroposteriorly long (0) or short and centered on neural arch, giving arch an “X” shape in dorsal view (1)) is taken directly from Makovicky and Sues (1998), where it diagnosed oviraptorosaurs. An earlier version of the character also involving neural spine height was used by Makovicky (1995) to help diagnose a clade containing not only oviraptorosaurs, but therizinosaurs, ornithomimosaurs and troodontids. Makovicky in turn took it from Russell and Dong (1994), who used the X-shaped neural arch as part of their therizinosauroid diagnosis. Norell et al. score the character as present in all coelurosaurs, though oddly score tyrannosaurids inapplicable. Coding is problematic since neural spine length is difficult to quantify and compare between taxa. While neural arch length is a convenient standard, the length and angle of zygapophyses serve as confounding variables. Neural spines usually rise gradually from the arch making their edges hard to define, especially when many low-spined taxa have mere bumps or ridges. Length also varies throughout the cervical column, with some vertebrae having spines over twice as long as others. While usually posterior spines are shorter, taxa such as Alvarezsaurus and Deinonychus show this is not always the case. Thus while Makovicky and Sues used Microvenator's short neural spine as an example of their oviraptorosaurian morphology, compared to neural arch length (between zygapophyses, ignoring epipophyses) the single measurable posterior cervical has a neural spine of similar length to those on posterior cervicals of carnosaurs and Deinonychus. Yet the latter two taxa are classic examples of long neural spines. No measure could be found which separates coelurosaurs, oviraptorosaurs or Makovicky's coelurosaur subgroup from other taxa, so instead the arbitrary measure of a neural spine <30% of neural arch length on cervicals 3-5 is provisionally used here for this character. In this case, Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Velociraptor (pers. obs. of IGM 100/976), Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1974) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have long neural spines. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) have short neural spines. Chirostenotes' anterior cervical neural spine is started to be long (Sues, 1997) so is provisionally recoded. Troodon's (Makovicky, 1995), Sinornithosaurus' (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Saurornitholestes' (Makovicky, 1995) have yet to be illustrated, so are conservatively recoded unknown. Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Microvenator (pers. obs.) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) do not preserve anterior cervical neural spines.

102. Ornitholestes (Makovicky, 1995), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have single pairs of cervical pleurocoels."Ingenia" (Makovicky, 1995) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) have two pairs. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) are polymorphic. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Chure, 2000) and Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) have mostly single pairs, but double pairs on at least one side of some centra, so are also coded as polymorphic here. The high variability of this character in a single specimen or even single vertebra mixed with the fact very few taxa have completely preserved cervical columns suggests it may not be very useful. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994; though they state this is like other ornithomimosaurs, Britt and Makovicky have shown this is a misconception in ornithomimids), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005; axis at least) and Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) should be coded inapplicable as they lack cervical pleurocoels.

103. This character only gives two options for the cervical and anterior dorsal centra- amphiplatyan (0) and opisthocoelous (1). Yet there are many other morphologies found in coelurosaurs (amphicoelous, platycoelous, procoelous, heterocoelous), so it is here revised to indicate a concave or flat anterior articular surface (0) or a convex one (1). As only the first one or two dorsals are opisthocoelous in Sinraptor and Allosaurus, only cervicals are here used for this character. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Erlikosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (Makovicky, 1995), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Ji et al., 2005), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) have a concave or flat surface. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997) has a convex surface. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916) and Saurornitholestes (Makovicky, 1995) are polymorphic. Confuciusornis is left scored unknown, as it has heterocoelous anterior surfaces. Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) doesn't preserve cervicals, so is not coded here.

104. Here, a prominent hypapophysis is defined as one over 25% of central height. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) and Archaeopteryx (Chiappe, 2001) have small hypapophyses. Rinchenia (unpublished photo), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007), Deinonychus (Norell and Makovicky, 1999), Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) and Confuciusornis (coding in Chiappe, 2001) have large hyapophyses. "Ingenia" has been given conflicting codings by Lu (2004) and Maryanska et al. (2002), so is retained as unknown here. Microvenator (pers. obs.) does not preserve all dorsal centra, so cannot be said to definitely lack large hypapophyses.

105. This character is here quantified as having a parapophysis length >20% of central width. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000) and Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) have short posterior dorsal parapophyses. Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) and Rahonavis (unpublished photo) have long parapophyses. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) and Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976) are both polymorphic.

106. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), Saurornithoides (pers. obs. of AMNH 6516), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) have dorsal hyposphene-hypantra.

107. This character is stated poorly as "Cervical vertebrae but not dorsal vertebrae pneumatic (0), or all presacral vertebrae pneumatic (1)". This doesn't allow the coding of taxa without cervical pleurocoels (e.g. Mononykus) or those with cervical and anterior dorsal pleurocoels (most taxa). Note too that though stated as vertebral pneumaticity, it only refers to central pneumaticity, as every coelurosaur has pneumatic dorsal neural arches. The character is restated here as "Some dorsal vertebrae lack pleurocoels (0); all dorsal vertebrae have pleurocoels (1)", which matches Norell et al.'s coding. While most taxa don't preserve all dorsal centra, since no taxon is known to lack pleurocoels anteriorly and have them posteriorly, and no taxon has more than five pleurocoelous dorsals without having them all pleurocoelous, any specimen preserving a pleurocoelous posterior dorsal is here coded 1. Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) and Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) have some non-pleurocoelous dorsals. Oviraptor (unpublished photos), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999; assuming homology with pygostylians deep lateral fossae) have all dorsals pleurocoelous.

108. This a composite character involving anterior dorsal transverse process length (0- long, 1- short), shape (0- thin, 1- wide) and inclination (1- slightly inclined). Only length is coded here, measured as the distance the transverse process extends laterally past the prezygapophysis, divided by the distance between the midline of the vertebra and the outer edge of the prezygapophysis. As length varies throughout the column, the quantification is different for each vertebra- <180% for dorsal 1, <200% for 2, <215% for 3 and <250% for 4. However, this does not match Norell et al.'s codings, as Gallimimus, Deinonychus and Achillobator all have the derived condition. Thus only taxa which can be directly measured are coded, not those which have only been coded in a matrix. Caudipteryx (Zhou, 2000) is primitive. Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) are coded as derived. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos) is polymorphic, as dorsal 3 has short processes, but dorsals 1and 2 do not. Gorgosaurus, Struthiomimus, Shuvuuia, Saurornitholestes and Velociraptor are conservatively recoded unknown.

109. Here a dorsal neural spine table is quantified as an apical transverse expansion >150% of minimum spine width. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999), Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Unenlagia (unpublished photos) and Rahonavis (unpublished photo) lack dorsal spine tables. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have spine tables. Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) are polymorphic. As with many vertebral characters, many other taxa may be polymorphic as well, but positional variation and a lack of preserved/described vertebrae makes it difficult to determine.

110. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Mononykus (unpublished photos), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Oviraptor (Makovicky, 1995) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have intervertebral ligament scars which extend to the tips of their dorsal neural spines. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Rahonavis (unpublished photo) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have scars which end far below the neural spine tip.

111. State 2 should be "7 or more" sacral vertebrae, not "8 or more", since 7 sacrals is otherwise unrepresented in the states and that's how many sacrals Confuciusornis (coded as 2) has. Adasaurus (Senter, pers. comm.) has five sacrals. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Segnosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Sinornithoides (coding in Senter, 2003), Saurornithoides (pers. obs. of AMNH 6516), Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1976) and Unenlagia (Novas and Puerta, 1997) have six sacrals. Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Avimimus (Makovicky, 1995), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (coding in Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1983) have more than six sacrals. Archaeopteryx is polymorphic, with the Thermopolis specimen having six sacrals (Mayr et al., 2007).

112. Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) has a ventrally rounded posterior sacral centra. Ornitholestes (Makovicky, 1995), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Microvenator (Makovicky, 1995), IGM 100/42 (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007), Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998), Unenlagia (Rauhut, 2003) and Rahonavis (unpublished photo) have ventrally flattened and/or grooved posterior sacral centra. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997) and Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) have ventrally keeled posterior sacral centra. Alvarezsaurus (Novas, 1996) should be coded polymorphic, since sacrals 2-4 are grooved ventrally, but sacrals 4 and 5 have ventral keels. Similarly, in Troodon sacrals 1-4 are flattened ventrally, 2-5 are grooved ventrally, and 5 has a keel posteriorly. In Garudimimus sacrals 4 and 6 are grooved, but 5 is keeled (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005). "Ingenia" varies, with IGM 100/30 having ventral grooves and 100/33 having ventral keels (Lu, 2004). Archaeopteryx (Novas, 1996) and Confuciusornis (coding in Chiappe, 2001) at least lack ventral keels.

113. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1974) lack sacral pleurocoels. Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Adasaurus (Kubota and Barsbold, 2006), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999; again assuming the fossae are homologous) have only anterior sacral pleurocoels. Gorgosaurus (Britt, 1993), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) have all sacral centra pleurocoelous. Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Microvenator (Makovicky, 1995), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) and Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) at least lack some sacral pleurocoels. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) at least have some pleurocoelous sacrals.

114. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Rahonavis (coding in Senter, 2007) lack a convex posterior sacral articular surface. Oviraptor (Smith, 1992) does not preserve sacral vertebrae.

115. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) lack a zygapophyseal ridge on the sacral neural platform. Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a ridge. Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988; Sues, 1997) and Velociraptor (absent in pers. obs. of IGM 100/986; present in IGM 100/985- Norell and Makovicky, 1997) are polymorphic.

116. This character is problematic, as it involves both the elongation of distal caudals and reduction of their transverse processes. Yet even oviraptorids exhibit these features, merely at a less extreme degree than most other theropods. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Avimimus (Watabe et al., 2000 reported elongate caudals), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) have a caudal transition point. Microvenator (pers. obs.) preserves a distal caudal 2.7 times longer than tall and with highly reduced transverse processes, so is here coded as having a transition point. Alxasaurus' caudal centra are all less than twice as long as tall, but caudals do lack transverse processes after the thirteenth as as many coelurosaurs (Russell and Dong, 1993). It is therefore coded polymorphic. Segnosaurus ("short, massive" distal caudals reported by Perle, 1979, though the presence of transverse processes remains unreported), Rinchenia (unpublished photos) and Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002) lack a caudal transition point. No distal caudals are known for Sinraptor dongi (Currie and Zhao, 1994) or Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), so they must be coded unknown. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable due to its pygostyle.

117. Ornitholestes (Makovicky, 1995), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Zanabazar (Barsbold, 1974; Norell et al., 2009), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) have more than ten caudals with transverse processes. Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) is tentatively coded as having a distally placed transition point, as its most distal preserved caudal is similar to the fifteenth in Nomingia and has a large transverse process. Similarly, Microvenator (pers. obs.) preserves an elongate caudal with transverse processes, so is coded that way. Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) has a proximally placed transition point. As noted above, Rinchenia and Conchoraptor lack a transition point, so should be coded as inapplicable. No distal caudals are known for Sinraptor dongi (Currie and Zhao, 1994), so it must be coded unknown. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable due to its pygostyle.

118. This character is a composite of proximal caudal centrum shape (0- oval; 1- box-like; 2- ?), transverse compression (0- tall; 1- ?; 2- laterally compressed) and presence of a ventral keel (yes in 2, presumably no in 0 and 1). As the ventral keel has the same distribution as those on the posterior sacrals which was coded for character 112, 118 is here restricted to shape. This means that Patagonykus (Novas, 1997) and Mononykus (unpublished photo) are recoded to reflect their oval articular surfaces, while Alvarezsaurus and Shuvuuia are coded unknown until their shape is reported. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Avimimus (Makovicky, 1995) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have round proximal caudal articular surfaces. Alxasaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) have rectangular proximal caudal centra.

119. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) lack accessory neural spines on their mid caudals. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) and Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) have accessory spines. There are too few caudals preserved in Microvenator (pers. obs.), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Zanabazar (Barsbold, 1974), Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) to rule out some having accessory spines. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable due to its pygostyle.

120. This character should be ordered, as a flat dorsal vertebral surface is intermediate between a convex and a concave one. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Avimimus (coding in Makovicky, 1995), Rinchenia (Barsbold et al., 2000) and "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 2000) have a neural spine on their distal caudals. Sinornithosaurus (coding Xu and Zhang, 2005) has a flat dorsal surface. Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004) have a dorsal sulcus on their distal caudals. Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable due to its pygostyle.

121. This character should be ordered, since state 0 has a prezygapophyseal length intermediate between states 1 and 2.
