This means the states must be switched though, so they are redefined here as "longest distal caudal prezygapophysis <33% of centrum length (0); 33-100% (1); >100% (2)." Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) and Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004) have short prezygapophyses. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A) and Microvenator (pers. obs.) have long prezygapophyses. Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) has extremely elongate prezygapophyses. Utahraptor can only be coded as 1/2 based on described materials (Kirkland et al., 1993). Confuciusornis should be coded inapplicable due to its pygostyle.

122. This character has overlap in states, with 25 caudals being covered by both states 1 and 2. Here, 25 caudals is covered by state 1, with state 2 being 24 or less. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) and Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) have over 40 caudals. Ornitholestes (Osborn, 1916), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Conchoraptor (Barsbold et al., 2000), "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 2000) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) have 25-40 caudals. Shuvuuia preserves 20 caudals and the distalmost suggests more than five more were present (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), so is coded 0/1. Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979) also has over 25 caudals. Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) only preserves 13 caudals, and while its total was probably around 24 based on comparison to Archaeopteryx, it is conservatively coded 1/2 here.

123. This character is problematic for several reasons. Comparing classic plesiomorphic and apomorphic taxa Allosaurus and Deinonychus, the dorsal end of proximal chevrons in the latter are not anteroposteriorly longer or more flattened. Nor is the shaft of the former ever cynlindrical. Instead, both have a short dorsal end which is transversely wider than long, and around the level of the haemal canal becomes transversely compressed. The illusion of anteroposterior elongation in Deinonychus is caused by the dorsoventrally short shaft. However, this varies in every taxon throughout the caudal series, universally becoming dorsoventrally shorter distally. Note any measurement of dorsal anteroposterior length will also be affected by the size of dorsoanterior and dorsoposterior processes. The character is here defined as "dorsal anteroposterior length of chevrons >75% of dorsoventral depth by caudal 10". Regardless of how exactly one defines it though, Norell et al.'s codings are in error by placing Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972) and Shuvuuia (pers. obs of IGM 100/975) among the derived group. Also having dorsoventrally elongate proximal chevrons are Ornitholestes (Osborn, 1916), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004). Having shortened chevrons are Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005). Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) does not preserve proximal chevrons.

124. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005- the bifurcate processes seems to be the normally bifurcate anterodorsal processes), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) and Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) have anteriorly simple distal chevrons. Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) has anteriorly bifurcate distal chevrons.

125. Note I do not include the axial or tenth cervical rib in this character, as they are often longer than their centra even in taxa with otherwise reduced cervical ribs (e.g. Gallimimus, Caudipteryx). Sinraptor (Currie and Dong, 1994), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and Velociraptor (pers. obs. of IGM 100/976) have cervical ribs longer than their associated centra. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2003), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) have short cervical ribs. Tsaagan is polymorphic, with cervical 4 having a rib shorter than its centrum (unless the former is broken), while cervicals 7-9 have ribs longer than their centra. As with many other vertebral characters, this is usually only known for a few ribs of each taxon, so may actually be polymorphic in other taxa as well. Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) does not preserve cervical ribs, so cannot be coded. Shuvuuia is provisionally left coded as having short ribs, though cervical ribs were not observed in IGM 100/975 or 100/99.

126. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Pelecanimimus (Sereno et al., 2008) and Garudimimus (Kobayashi, 2004) lack ossified uncinate processes. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Conchoraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Saurornitholestes (Codd, 2004) and Deinonychus (Paul, 2002) have ossified uncinate processes. Confuciusornis is polymorphic (Chiappe et al., 1999).

127. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917) and Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) lack ossified sternal ribs. Pelecanimimus (Senter, pers. comm.), Caudipteryx (Zhou and Wang, 2000), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Deinonychus (Paul, 2002) have ossified sternal ribs.

128. The codings of this character are reversed in the matrix compared to their description in the text. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have medial gastralia longest. Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) have lateral gastralia longest.

129. Sinraptor, Shuvuuia, Mononykus and Confuciusornis are coded as having state 2, which is not defined but which is probably the presence a sternal keel. This is followed here, and the character is ordered since taxa with keeled sterna also have fused sterna. Adasaurus (Jasinoski et al., 2006) has unfused sternal plates. Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2009) has fused but keeless sternal plates. "Ingenia" is polymorphic (Barsbold, 1984), with some specimens having fused but keeless sterna. Confuciusornis is also polymorphic (Chiappe et al., 1999), having fused sterna which are sometimes keeled. Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Gorgosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1981), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) and Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer and Tischlinger, 2004- the supposed sternum is a coracoid) are coded inapplicable since they lack ossified sterna.

130. Pelecanimimus (Senter, pers. comm.) and Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000) lack lateral sternal processes. As in the previous character, Allosaurus, Gorgosaurus, Tyrannosaurus, Struthiomimus, Sinornithoides and Archaeopteryx are coded inapplicable.

131. This character is correlated with 139, which codes the medially inflected coracoids of paravians. The only taxa coded differently for each are IGM 100/42 (which contra Norell et al., does have an inflected coracoid- unpublished photos) and Gorgosaurus (whose supposed sternum with anteriorly facing coracoid facets is a mass of fused gastralia- Brochu, 2003). Consequently, the character is deleted and all fifteen coded taxa are recoded ?.

132. As with 128, the codings of this character are reversed compared to the description. Pelecanimimus (Senter, pers. comm.) and Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2009) have coracoid grooves in their sternum. Sinraptor must be coded unknown, as a carnosaurian coracoid could only articulate on the anterolateral edges, which are not preserved. As in the other sternal characters, Allosaurus, Gorgosaurus, Tyrannosaurus, Struthiomimus, Sinornithoides and Archaeopteryx are coded inapplicable.

133. Tyrannosaurus (Lipkin et al., 2007) and Sinornithosaurus (unpublished photo) lack a hypocleidium. Gorgosaurus (Makovicky and Currie, 1998) and Velociraptor (pers. obs. of IGM 100/976) have a hypocleidium. Allosaurus (Chure and Madsen, 1996) is polymorphic. Caudipteryx should be coded unknown, as the furculae are all preserved in lateral view without a visible symphysis (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A; Zhou et al., 2000). Sinornithoides (Nesbitt et al., 2009) should be coded unknown, as the clavicles are broken medially (not unfused, as Norell et al.'s inapplicable coding would suggest). Pelecanimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Gallimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1985) and Shuvuuia (Chiappe et al., 2002) should all be coded inapplicable since they seem to lack ossified clavicles.

134. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2003), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Segnosaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) lack an everted acromion. Avimimus (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Oviraptor (unpublished photos), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (unpublished photos), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Troodon (Jasinoski, 2003), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999), Rahonavis (unpublished photo) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a laterally everted acromion. Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998) does not preserve a scapula, so cannot be coded.

135. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Oviraptor (Smith, 1992) have a humerus shorter than their scapula. Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski, 2003) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969; Paul, 2002- even considering the incomplete proximal scapula of AMNH 3015) have a longer humerus. Avimimus' holotype scapula is incomplete (Kurzanov, 1981), while more complete specimens have yet to be described. Sinornithoides' scapula is also incomplete (Currie and Dong, 2001). The humerus of Sinraptor is unpreserved (Currie and Zhao, 1994), as is the scapula of Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005).

136. Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1981), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack a deep subglenoid fossa. Troodon (Jasinoski, 2003) and Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski, 2003) have a deep subglenoid fossa.

137. Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Microvenator (pers. obs.), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Troodon (Jasinoski, 2003), Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski, 2003) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) have an unfused scapulocoracoid. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1981) and Adasaurus (Kubota and Barsbold, 2006) have a fused scapulocoracoid. Tyrannosaurus (fused in FMNH PR2081- Brochu, 2003), Struthiomimus (unfused in UCMZ 1980.1- Nicholls and Russell, 1985), Caudipteryx (fused in BPM 0001- Zhou et al., 2000) and Archaeopteryx (fused in London- Petronievics and Woodward, 1917) are polymorphic.

138. This is a composite character involving coracoid anteroventral corner development (0- subcircular; 1- subquadrangular; 2- ?), depth (0- shallow; 1- extensive; 2- shallow) and posterior process length (0- ?; 1- ?; 2- elongate). The development of an anterior corner is gradual (e.g. Chirostenotes is hard to catagorize) and difficult to quantify, while the posterior process is also elongate in maniraptoriforms with deep coracoids (e.g. IGM 100/42, Deinonychus) but not as obvious as those with shallow blades. Thus the character is here redefined to code for only coracoid depth. Specifically, a coracoid proximodistally >90% of its anteroposterior length (excluding posteroventral process) is defined as state 1. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975) and Mononykus (unpublished photos) are recoded 1, while Pelecanimimus, Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972) and Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1985) are recoded 0. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) has a shallow coracoid. Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1981), Microvenator (pers. obs.), Oviraptor (unpublished photo), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), Saurornitholestes (Rauhut, 2003), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006), Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.) have a deep coracoid.

139. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), "Ingenia" (unpublished photos), Sinornithoides (Jasinoski, 2003), Troodon (Jasinoski, 2003), Saurornitholestes (Jesinoski, 2003) and Tsaagan (coding in Turner et al., 2007) have an inflected coracoid. Conchoraptor is provisionally coded unknown, based on Norell et al.'s miscoding of other oviraptorids.

140. This character is redefined as in Senter (2007) to indicate the extent of the glenoid on the external scapular surface, as this is codable from disarticulated scapulae. While glenoids which don't extend on the external surface may be directed laterally in life (e.g. IGM 100/42), this is due to curvature and orientation of the pectoral girdle and is very difficult to judge in most specimens. Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2009), Rinchenia (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), "Ingenia" (unpublished photos), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999), Unenlagia (Novas and Puerta, 1997) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) have posteroventrally oriented glenoids. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1985), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979), Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski, 2003), Tsaagan (Norell et al., 2006) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) have laterally oriented glenoids. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988) and Microvenator (pers. obs.) cannot be coded, as they do not preserve a scapula.

141. This is a composite character involving deltopectoral crest depth (0- large; 1- less pronounced; 2- very weakly developed; 3-?; 4- extremely broad), shape (0- distinct, proximal end of humerus quadrangular in anterior view; 1- forming an arc rather than being quadrangular; 2- proximal end of humerus with rounded edges; 3- ?; 4- triangular in anterior view) and proximodistal length (0-?; 1-?; 2-?;3- extremely long; 4-?). However, deltopectoral crest depth is largely an illusion caused by humeral elongation. Most coelurosaurs have crests approximately equal to the shaft's minimum width (Mononykus and Confuciusornis being exceptions among the present taxa), and scaling even seemingly divergent taxa like Gallimimus and Shuvuuia to the same distal width results in crests of similar depth. Thus crest depth is ignored here. As the amount of roundedness is difficult to quantify, instead the angle between the anterior/proximal and distal edges was used. This indicates no separation between ornithomimosaurs (coded 2 by Norell et al.) and the taxa they code 1, and indeed the state descriptions do not differ as an arc is a rounded edge. The character is ordered and states reordered since a quadrangular crest is intermediate between a triangular and low angled crest. The new states are- >95 degrees (0); 50-95 degrees (1); <50 degrees (2). State 3 is coded as present in alvarezsaurids (though Patagonykus doesn't preserve the crest, so should be coded unknown), but since Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975) has an angle of ~90 degrees and Mononykus (unpublished photos) has an angle of 130 degrees, they are recoded 1 and 0 respectively. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski et al., 2006) have a crest angled between 50 and 95 degrees. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002; may be ontogenetic) and Sinornithosaurus (unpublished photo) have a low angled crest. Oviraptor's crest is broken proximally, but is less than 90 degrees at least so can be coded 1/2.

142. Ornitholestes (unpublished photo), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos) and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) lack a lateral ridge on the deltopectoral crest. Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Saurornitholestes (Jasinoski et al., 2006) have a ridge. Caudipteryx is polymorphic (0- Zhou and Wang, 2000; 1- Zhou et al., 2000). Patagonykus (Novas, 1997) does not preserve the deltopectoral crest, so cannot be coded.

143. This is here quantified as an olecranon which projects past the humeral cotyle more than a third of the length of the rest of the element. Segnosaurus (unpublished photo) and Saurornitholestes (Novas, 1998) have a short olecranon process. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) does not preserve an ulna.

144. Troodon (Russell, 1969), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a transversely flat distal ulna.

145. Ornitholestes (Senter, 2006), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) lack an intercotylar crest on the proximal ulna. Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Caudipteryx (coding in Sereno, 1999), Troodon (Russell, 1969), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Gishlick, 2003) and Archaeopteryx (coding in Gishlick, 2002) have an intercotylar crest on the proximal ulna.

146. Theropod ulnares are rarely preserved and often disarticulated, making Norell et al.'s division between proximally quadrangular and triangular elements difficult to substantiate. Allosaurus (Chure, 2001) preserves a lateral distal carpal Chure calls the intermedium, but which is probably an ulnare as the latter element was not otherwise present. It is roughly trapezoidal in proximal view, while in distal view it is transversely narrow with a squared extensor edge and round flexor edge. Gorgosaurus' ulnare (Lambe, 1917) is more rounded but slightly trapezoidal in proximal view. The supposed ulnare of Tyrannosaurus MOR 555 (Carpenter and Smith, 2001) is part of a fused distal carpal I and II. Harpymimus' ulnare is described as being triangular in distal view (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005). Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1985) was described as having a pisiform, which has the position of an avian ulnare. It is triangular in extensor view as in Allosaurus, but semilunate in distal view, differing from Allosaurus where the extensor edge is squared. The orientation of Alxasaurus' supposed ulnare is unknown and while triangular in the illustrated views (probably flexor, extensor and proximal/distal), it could have a pointed or flat extensor edge depending on the reconstruction. Caudipteryx preserves an ulnare overlapping the radius of NGMC 94-4-A (Gishlick, 2002), which matches the shape of Allosaurus' in lateral view. The supposed ulnare in IVPP V12430 (Zhou et al., 2000) is probably a distal carpal II. Sinornithosaurus (Gishlick, 2002) preserves a disarticulated ulnare which is similar to Caudipteryx's in shape so is probably also in lateral view. The Eichstatt Archaeopteryx's (Wellnhofer, 1974) ulnare is roughly oval in the left manus and smaller and triangular in the right, which matches the condition in Allosaurus when seen in flexor and extensor views respectively. The Munich specimen (Campbell, 2008) preserves an elongate section with a pointed tip that may also be in extensor view. The ulnare in Confuciusornis (Goernemann, 1999) is triangular in extensor view, again matching Allosaurus. Thus neither Archaeopteryx or Confuciusornis can be coded for ulnare shape in proximal view. The ulnare is unknown in Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Velociraptor and Deinonychus (Gishlick, 2002). IGM 100/42 is not mounted with an ulnare, and is here provisionally left coded unknown due to Norell et al.'s otherwise problematic codings. While Norell et al. code Shuvuuia and Mononykus as inapplicable, Perle et al. (1994) noted a facet on metacarpal III indicated at least one missing carpal in the latter, and neither genus preserves most small manual elements. They are here recoded unknown.

147. Caudipteryx (Ji et al., 1998; Zhou et al., 2000) has unfused distal carpals, with distal carpal II being small and capping metacarpal II. Tyrannosaurus (Carpenter and Smith, 2001; misidentified as radiale and ulnare), Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Troodon (Russell, 1969; based on the semilunate having a large facet for metacarpal II) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) have fused distal carpals I and II. Allosaurus is polymorphic, as some specimens have fused carpals (Chure, 2001). Alvarezsaurids do not preserve separate carpals and fuse at least distal carpal I to metacarpal I. In Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), only the medial portion of the trochlear surface is preserved, making it uncertain what the relation to distal carpal II is. Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) has a complete trochlea on the medial part of metacarpal I, but two facets lateral to it which cover parts of metacarpal II. Distal carpal II may be fused to distal carpal I and reduced in extent, or may have articulated with one of the lateral facets. The situation is similar in Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), though metacarpal II is unpreserved and the facet obscured by sediment.

148. Note this does not affect the same elements in every taxon. In Patagonykus, Shuvuuia and Mononykus, distal carpal I (+II?) is fused to metacarpal I, while in Confuciusornis distal carpals I+II and fused to metacarpals II+III. Conchoraptor (Barsbold, 1986) lacks a carpometacarpus. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) has the semilunate fused to all three metacarpals. Oviraptor (pers. obs.) has metacarpals I and II fused to its semilunate. Since Archaeopteryx specimens are all young (Erickson et al., 2009), it is recoded unknown as fusion may have existed in adults.

149. This character is divided into three states. State 2 is defined as a semilunate carpal which "covers bases of all metacarpals" and is coded only for Mononykus and Shuvuuia. Yet as noted for character 147, the semilunate's trochlea ends on the medial portion of metacarpal I in Mononykus, while metacarpals II and III have additional facets, perhaps for other carpals. The condition in Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975) where the trochlea is also medially confined and metacarpals II and III are unfused to metacarpal I, indicates the semilunate did not extend to metacarpals II or III. Oddly most ornithomimosaurs, tyrannosaurids and Alxasaurus are coded inapplicable, though Harpymimus is not. These all have distal carpals homologous to a semilunate, so can be evaluated for their extent. While Confuciusornis is the only taxon coded as having a semilunate which is "small,
covering about half of base of metacarpals I and II", but it actually has an intermediate condition compared to the other taxa. The states are here quantified as "semilunate carpal covers >74% of proximal transverse width of metacarpals I and II (0); covers <75% (1)." Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002) have large distal carpals. Allosaurus (Chure, 2001), Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Carpenter and Smith, 2001), Pelecanimimus (Perez-Moreno et al., 1994), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Struthiomimus (Nicholls and Russell, 1985), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975; metacarpal II width assumed to be similar to Mononykus), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2002; supposed ulnare is distal carpal II) and Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) have small distal carpals. Confuciusornis is polymorphic, as some specimens (e.g. Goernemann, 1999) have large carpals. Gallimimus does not preserve distal carpals, so cannot be coded. Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991) does not preserve carpal or metacarpal elements. Patagonykus only preserves metacarpal I (and a fragment of II of unreported width), of which only the medial part of the trochlea is preserved, which merely shows it extended at least 65% over the width of metacarpal I.

150. This character should be ordered, but the states need to be switched so that the intermediate state is 1. The new quantified states are "metacarpal I <50% of metacarpal II in length (0); 50-75% (1); >75% (2). Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2009) and Oviraptor (Osborn, 1924) have a short metacarpal I. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Chirostenotes (Currie, 1990) and Conchoraptor (Barsbold, 1986) have an intermediate metacarpal I. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917; Matthew and Brown, 1923) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) are polymorphic, with ratios below and above 50%. Shuvuuia cannot be coded, as no metacarpal II is preserved (pers. obs. IGM 100/975).

151. Avimimus (Tsuihiji et al., 2009), Oviraptor (pers. obs.) and Conchoraptor (Barsbold, 1986) have phalanges on manual digit III. Shuvuuia specimen IGM 100/975 (pers. obs.) preserves a single phalanx from a lateral digit, but whether it is from II or III is unknown, as is the morphology of metacarpal III. Mononykus has a ball-shaped articular surface on metacarpal III (Perle et al., 1994), but whether this bore an ossified phalanx is unknown.

152. This is a composite character involving manual ungual curvature (0- strongly curved; 1- weakly curved or straight), flexor tubercle size (0- large; 1- weak) and flexor tubercle proximodistal placement (0- ?; 1- displaced distally from articular end). All of these characters also vary between digits in most taxa. Here, the curvature of manual ungual I is used. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.) and Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979) have strongly curved unguals. Tyrannosaurus (Carpenter and Smith, 2001), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A and 97-4-A) and Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1974) have weakly curved unguals. As only ungual III is preserved in Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), it is here recoded unknown. Only one ungual is partially preserved in Rinchenia (unpublished photos) and Troodon (Russell, 1969), which do not allow us to estimate curvature since the distal portion is missing. The supposed manual unguals of Utahraptor are pedal ungual II (Britt et al., 2001), so it cannot be coded.

153. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987; based on the metacarpal widths), Chirostenotes (Currie, 1990) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999; based on phalanx I-1) have a non-hypertrophied manual ungual I. Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) does not preserve manual material.

154. Note this is present in different digits of different taxa, so may not be strictly homologous. Thus I follow Senter (2007) in separating each digit and using this character only for manual ungual I. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Pelecanimimus (coding in Senter, 2007), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-4-A and 97-9-A; Zhou et al., 2000), "Ingenia" (Barsbold, 1984), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) have ungual I unlipped. Archaeopteryx (Wellnhofer, 1992, 1993) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999; Goernemann, 1999) are polymorphic. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Oviraptor (unpublished photos; all unguals lipped) and Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) have ungual I lipped. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994; ungual I unpreserved) and Troodon (Russell, 1969; at least one ungual unlipped) cannot be coded. The supposed manual unguals of Utahraptor are pedal ungual II (Britt et al., 2001), so it cannot be coded.

155. Only Alxasaurus and Segnosaurus are coded as having state 2 ("Ventral edge of anterior ala of ilium ... very strongly hooked"), but Segnosaurus' morphology is different than illustrated by Perle (Zanno, 2010). The ventral extent of both taxa's preacetabular process is no greater than taxa coded as weakly hooked by Norell et al., such as Gorgosaurus or Ornitholestes. Thus states 1 and 2 are combined here. The character is quantified by taking the ventral extent past the dorsalmost point of the ventral preacetabular edge, and dividing it by the depth of the preacetabular process at that point. While Norell et al.'s codings agree for those taxa which have values below 10% and above 40%, the taxa in the range of 10-40% are randomly divided between 0 and 1 (perhaps Norell et al. had a different means of quantification in mind). There is greatest agreement with Norell et al.'s codings if 20% is the ratio used to divide states 0 and 1. The final character used here is "ventral extent of anteroventral corner <20% of depth of preacetabular process (0); >19% (1)." Sinraptor (Currie and Dong, 1994), Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have poorly hooked preacetabular processes. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998), "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 1990), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Velociraptor (unpublished photos of IGM 100/25) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have strongly hooked processes. The preacetabular process is not preserved in Oviraptor (Smith, 1992), so it cannot be coded. Sinornithosaurus cannot be coded, as the dorsal extent of the ilium is hidden by the sacrum (Xu et al., 1999).

156. This is here quantified as a preacetabular process >120% the length of the postacetabular process. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001) and Adasaurus (Barsbold, 1983) have a short preacetabular process. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1976) have a long preacetabular process. The anterior edge of the preacetabular process in Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988) are unpreserved. The preacetabular process is not preserved in Oviraptor (Smith, 1992), so it cannot be coded.

157. This character is ordered by Norell et al. to have "anterior end strongly curved" the intermediate state between "anterior end of ilium gently rounded or straight" and "pointed at anterodorsal corner", which is not obviously true. If anything, a pointed morphology is intermediate, as the ventral preacetabular flange has an intermediate anteroposterior position compared to the anterior ilial edge. The states 0 and 1 are switched for this ordering assumption here. Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975) has a straight or slightly rounded anterior margin. Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999; based on the angle of the anterior edge) and Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001) have an anterodorsal corner. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) have a strongly rounded anterior edge. The anterior edge of the preacetabular process in Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988) is unpreserved.

158. This character should be ordered. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) have prominent supracetabular crests. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010), Unenlagia (Novas, 2004), Rahonavis (Makovicky et al., 2005) and Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) have low supracetabular crests. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1916), Caudipteryx (coding in Senter, 2007), Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), Saurornitholestes (coding in Senter, 2007) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) lack supracetabular crests. Alxasaurus is conservatively recoded 1/2, as Zanno (2010) codes it has having a low crest while Norell et al. (2001) code it as having an absent one.

159. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Oviraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) lack a supratrochanteric process. Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have a supratrochanteric process. Caudipteryx is polymorphic (0- pers. obs. NGMC 97-9-A; 1- Zhou et al., 2000).

160. Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Alvarezsaurus (Martinelli and Vera, 2007), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000; Zhou and Wang, 2000), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 1990) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have squared postacetabular processes. Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975; oddly coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002) and Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001) have pointed postacetabular processes. As Conchoraptor is coded differently by Maryanska et al. (2002), it is here coded unknown.

161. This is here quantified as coding for a divergence of the dorsal postacetabular margin over 10 degrees from parallel. Ornitholestes (unpublished photo), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975; oddly coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Unenlagia (Novas, 2004), Rahonavis (unpublished photo), Archaeopteryx (Paul, 2002) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have parallel postacetabular processes. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Alvarezsaurus (Novas, 1996; oddly coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Rinchenia (unpublished photo) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) have posteriorly diverging processes.

162. Shuvuuia (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009; oddly coded as nonexistant state 2 by Norell et al.), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a shallow to absent brevis fossa. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Rinchenia (unpublished photo) have a deep brevis fossa.

163. Chirostenotes (coding in Senter, 2007) and Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999) have a low or absent antitrochanter. Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1916), Ornitholestes (pers. obs.), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Rinchenia (unpublished photo), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), "Ingenia" (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Saurornitholestes (coding in Senter, 2007), Unenlagia (Novas, 2004), Rahonavis (Chiappe, 2002) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a prominent antitrochanter.

164. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Microvenator (AMNH online), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999), Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999), Unenlagia (Novas, 2004), Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Archaeopteryx (Ostrom, 1976) and Confuciusornis (Goernemann, 1999; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.) have a posteriorly extensive cuppedicus fossa. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Alvarezsaurus (Martinelli and Vera, 2007), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.) and "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996) have an anteriorly restricted cuppedicus fossa. Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969, 1976) is polymorphic. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) and Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976) both only have a medial ridge above the M. pubo-ischio-femoralis internus 1 origin, so cannot be coded for the posterior extent of any laterally visible cuppedicus fossa. Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) lack a cuppedicus fossa so must be coded inapplicable.

165. This character should be ordered. Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Rinchenia (unpublished photos), Deinonychus (Novas, 1996) and Unenlagia (Novas, 2004) and Rahonavis (unpublished photos) have a deep cuppedicus fossa. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Alvarezsaurus (Novas, 1996), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007), Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) and Confuciusornis (Goernemann, 1999) have a shallow cuppedicus fossa. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Madsen, 1976), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) lack a cuppedicus fossa. Alxasaurus was coded by Xu et al. (1999) as lacking a fossa, so is conservatively coded 1/2 here. The condition in Archaeopteryx (Ostrom, 1976) is difficult to determine, with the London and Berlin specimens both having a fossa, but of unknown lateral overhang. The same is true for Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001).

166. Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010) lacks a proximodorsal ischial process. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000; Zhou and Wang, 2000), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) and Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) have a proximodorsal ischial process. Chirostenotes is polymorphic, as RTMP 79.20.1 has a small proximodorsal process (Currie and Russell, 1988). Mononykus does not preserve an ischium (Perle et al., 1994), so cannot be coded. Alxasaurus' proximal ischium is unpreserved (Russell and Dong, 1994).

167. This is a composite character coding for anteroposterior ischial curvature (0- straight; 1- ventrodistally curved anteriorly; 2- ?; 3- ?), medial orientation of obturator process (0- ?; 1- ?; 2- twisted at midshaft and with flexure of obturator process toward midline so that distal end is horizontal; 3- ?) and lateral ischial curvature in section (0- straight; 1- ?; 2- ?; 3- laterally concave curvature in anterior view). State 2 is only coded in Velociraptor and Deinonychus, but many taxa have medially inclined obturator processes (e.g. Allosaurus, Suzhousaurus, Citipati, Saurornithoides) and no taxon has such extreme medial inclination that the distal ischium is horizontal. Lateral concavity is covered by Makovicky et al.'s (2005) character 168. Both characters are difficult to code for many taxa, and character 167 is here restricted to ventrodistal curvature. Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996), Saurornithoides (pers. obs.), Troodon (Hutchinson, 2001), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1997) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969) are recoded as lacking ventral curvature. IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo) is recoded as having ventral curvature. Avimimus does not preserve the distal ischium (Kurzanov, 1987), so cannot be coded for its curvature. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon), Adasaurus (Barsbold, 1983), Unenlagia (Gianechini and Apesteguia, 2011) and Confuciusornis (Goernemann, 1999) also lack ventral curvature and were not coded by Norell et al.. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994) and Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975) have a ventrally curved ischium. Tyrannosaurus is polymorphic, with some specimens (Osborn, 1906; 1916) having a curved shaft. The ischium of Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) is difficult to code, as the placement of the mid-dorsal process and obturator process combine to make it appear ventrally curved. As there is no shaft distal to the obturator process though, it may be better to score it inapplicable. Mononykus does not preserve an ischium (Perle et al., 1994), so cannot be coded.

168. This character should be ordered. It is here quantified so that a proximally placed process is <34% down the ischium, and a distally placed process is >66% down. Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) has a proximally placed process. Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) has a process placed near midshaft. Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010) and Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon) have a distally placed process. The distal end and much of the ventral margin of Avimimus' ischium is missing (Kurzanov, 1987), but enough is preserved to show the obturator process was not proximally placed if present. Mononykus does not preserve an ischium (Perle et al., 1994), so cannot be coded. Since character 170 also defines an obturator process, state 0 (obturator process of ischium absent) is removed and Shuvuuia and Confuciusornis are recoded inapplicable.

169. Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), Troodon (unpublished illustration), Utahraptor (unpublished illustration) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998; while incomplete, the obturator process would have to be uniquely long to contact the pubis) lack contact between the obturator process and pubis. Confuciusornis lacks an obturator process (Goernemann, 1999) so should be coded inapplicable. Mononykus does not preserve an ischium (Perle et al., 1994), so cannot be coded. Alxasaurus was not illustrated with contact by Russell and Dong (1994), who noted the tentatively identified ischia had an uncertain orientation and no pubes are known. It is conservatively coded unknown here.

170. This character has problems as coded. No included taxon has an ischial obturator foramen (state 1), so all the taxa coded 1 are probably supposed to be coded 2. State 2 is "notch or foramen absent", but most taxa coded this way actually do have an obturator notch, it's simply much wider due to the distally placed obturator process (unless the entire ischium is shortened as in Unenlagia). As the absence of a notch is synonymous with the absence of a process, taxa coded as lacking a process in character 168 are recoded inapplicable in that character. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Saurornithoides (pers. obs. of AMNH 6516), Troodon (Hutchinson, 2001), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Adasaurus (Barsbold, 1983), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon), Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1997), Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969), Utahraptor (unpublished illustration), Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999), Unenlagia (Gianichini and Apesteguia, 2011) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) have an obturator notch. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997) and Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) lack a foramen at least, but may or may not have a notch. Archaeopteryx is polymorphic, as the London specimen has a foramen (Ostrom, 1976), but most other specimens have notches (Mayr et al., 2007). Mononykus does not preserve an ischium (Perle et al., 1994), so cannot be coded.

171. The proximolateral ischial scar coded for in this character has been shown by Hutchinson (2001) to be present in most theropods when well preserved and homologous with the proximodorsal process of character 166. Of the taxa included by Norell et al., only Velociraptor and Saurornithoides seem to lack a scar or process, and this may be due to ontogeny and preservation respectively. Thus the character is deleted and all twenty coded taxa recoded unknown.

172. Note this feature is small and probably easily overlooked in reconstruction and preparation, so may be more widespread than realized (e.g. Ostrom did not illustrate it in Deinonychus in 1969, nor did Hutchinson in Troodon despite noting the specimen had it in the same paper). Caudipteryx (unpublished photo of NGMC 97-4-A), Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) lack an obturator tuberosity. Troodon (Hutchinson, 2001) has a tuberosity. The ventral edge of the proximal ischium is incomplete in Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), so it cannot be coded. The proximoventral portion of the ischium is missing in Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998), so it cannot be coded.

173. IGM 100/42 (unpublished photos) have an ischium >66% of pubic length. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Saurornithoides (pers. obs. AMNH 6516) have an ischium <66% of pubic length. Alxasaurus does not preserve pubes (Russell and Dong, 1994), so cannot be coded. Avimimus cannot be coded, as the ischium is distally incomplete so can only be said to be >58% of pubic length (Kurzanov, 1987).

174. Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2009), Caudipteryx (Ji et al., 1998), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and Saurornitholestes (Holtz, 2001; as Troodon) have an unfused ischial symphysis. Unenlagia (coding in Agnolin and Novas, 2011) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have separated ischia.

175. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010), Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) and Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.) have an expanded distal ischium. Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004) and Saurornitholestes (Hutchinson, 2001; as cf. Troodon) lack an expanded distal ischium.

176. This character is here quantified as a proximal pubic shaft which projects <66 degrees from anterior (0); 67-120 degrees (1); >120 degrees (2). Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) is propubic. Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998), Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) and Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002) are mesopubic. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) are either pro- or mesopubic. Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1976) is either meso- or opisthopubic. Alxasaurus does not preserve pubes (Russell and Dong, 1994), so cannot be coded.

177. This character should be ordered. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Adasaurus (unpublished photo) and Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) have a significant anterior pubic boot. Rahonavis (unpublished photos) lacks an anterior pubic boot. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005) has at least a posterior boot, though the anterior extent is less certain. Sinornithoides (Russell and Dong, 1994) lacks a pubic boot.

178. This is a composite character involving anteroposterior flattening of the pubic shaft and anteroposterior placement of the pubic apron on the shaft. However, the former character is problematic in that it varies throughout the shaft. In both Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920) and Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1997, 1999), the shaft is proximally compressed transversely but becomes broad once the pubic apron begins, only to be transversely compressed once more distally at the pubic boot. While measuring shaft width separate from the apron is impossible due to the gradual boundary with the latter, Allosaurus' pubis is broader both proximal to and at the pubic apron. The difference thus seems due to the anteroposterior depth, which can be more easily measured compared to pubic length. This shows that anteroposterior shaft depth and symphysis placement are independent, as taxa like Patagonykus and Avimimus have narrow shafts but posteriorly placed symphyses. The symphysis placement is coded here. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (unpublished photo), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) and "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) lack an anteriorly placed symphysis. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Harpymimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Sinornithosaurus (unpublished photo), Rahonavis (unpublished photo), Archaeopteryx (Ostrom, 1976) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have an anteriorly placed symphysis.

179. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997) have a straight or posteriorly concave pubis. Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003) and Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010) have an anteriorly strongly concave pubis. Only the proximal pubis is preserved in Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), so it cannot be coded. The pubis is unknown in Rinchenia (unpublished photos), so it cannot be coded.

180. Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Ornitholestes (unpublished photo), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Chirostenotes (Sues, 1997), IGM 100/42 (unpublished illustration), "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Archaeopteryx (de Beer, 1954) have a long pubic apron. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010; unpublished photo) and Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) have a short pubic apron. Only the proximal pubis is preserved in Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994; coded inapplicable by Norell et al.), so it cannot be coded.

181. Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Mononykus (unpublished photos), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Unenlagia (unpublished photos) and Rahonavis (unpublished photo) lack a capital ligament fossa. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (Kim et al., 2005) and Deinonychus (Brinkman et al., 1998) have a capital ligament fossa.

182. This character should be ordered. It can be quantified by dividing the depth of the intertrochanteric notch (from the tip of the anterior trochanter) by the minimum femoral anteroposterior diameter. A "small groove" which is <66% of shaft diameter matches best with Norell et al.'s codings. Adasaurus (Kim et al., 2005) has a deep groove between the anterior and greater trochanters. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Mononykus (unpublished photos), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have a small groove between the anterior and greater trochanters. Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) has a fused trochanteric crest. Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988) was described as having an unusually distal anterior trochanter, but compared to taxa such as Caudipteryx, this is more likely to be the accessory trochanter. In that case, the anterior trochanter would be mostly or completely fused to the greater trochanter, resulting in a coding of 1/2. The same seems to be true for Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010). Utahraptor has an anterior trochanter of unknown separation (Britt et al., 2001), though not fused. The same is true for the incomplete anterior trochanter of Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991). The amount of separation is not preserved in Shuvuuia (pers. obs.).

183. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004) and Unenlagia (unpublished photos) have wing-like anterior trochanters. Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991) and Utahraptor (Britt et al., 2001) have cylindrical anterior trochanters. "Ingenia" is recoded as polymorphic, as IGM 100/33 has a wing-like anterior trochanter (Lu, 2004). Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994; supposed anterior trochanter is accessory trochanter), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010; ditto), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Rahonavis (unpublished photo) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) should be coded inapplicable, as the anterior trochanter is not identifiable as a distinct structure. The anterior trochanter is not preserved in Shuvuuia (pers. obs.). Byronosaurus cannot be coded as it does not preserve the proximal femur (Makovicky et al., 2003).

184. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Harpymimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (coding in Zanno, 2010), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (Kim et al., 2005) and Rahonavis (unpublished photos) lack posterior trochanters. Confuciusornis (Hutchinson, 2001) has a posterior trochanter.

185. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979), Adasaurus (Kim et al., 2005) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) have a fourth trochanter. Ornitholestes (Carpenter et al., 2005), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Rahonavis (unpublished photo) lack a fourth trochanter. Velociraptor was scored as polymorphic, but IGM 100/982 and 100/986 both have one (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) and no specimen has been described without one.

186. Alvarezsaurus (Bonaparte, 1991), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (Kim et al., 2005), Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) and Unenlagia (unpublished photo) lack an accessory trochanter. Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994; as anterior trochanter), Segnosaurus (Zanno, 2010; as anterior trochanter), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988; as anterior trochanter) and "Ingenia" (Psihoyos, 1996) have an accessory trochanter.

187. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Rinchenia (coding in Lu, 2004), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) lack an anteromedial crest on the distal femur. Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Gorgosaurus (Lambe, 1917), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004), Mononykus (unpublished photos), Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Microvenator (AMNH online), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), "Ingenia" (unpublished photo) and Unenlagia (unpublished photos) have an anteromedial crest on the distal femur.

188. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988) and Utahraptor (coding in Senter, 2007) lack an enclosed popliteal fossa. Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002) and Confuciusornis (coding in Clarke and Norell, 2002) have an enclosed fossa. Described Shuvuuia specimens don't preserve the distal popliteal area (pers. obs.; Longrich and Currie, 2009). Oviraptor (Smith, 1992) does not preserve the hindlimb, so cannot be coded.

189. The fibula in Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Conchoraptor (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Troodon (Russell, 1969), Sinornithosaurus (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005) and Saurornitholestes (Rauhut, 2003) reaches the proximal tarsals. Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) lack a fibular-tarsal articulation. "Ingenia" is polymorphic, as Lu (2004) indicates the fibula does not reach the proximal tarsals in IGM 100/33. Described Shuvuuia specimens don't preserve the fibula or calcaneum (pers. obs.; Longrich and Currie, 2009). The distal fibula and proximal tarsals of Alxasaurus are unpreserved (Russell and Dong, 1994).

190. This and the following character both describe the proximomedial concavity of the fibula, with 189 specifying the absence of a fossa and 190 the presence of a deep fossa. They are therefore combined here as- proximomedial fibular fossa absent (0); shallow (1); deep (2) (ordered). The fibular fossa is deep in Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Chure, 2000), Tyrannosaurus (Brochu, 2003), and Garudimimus (coding in Kobayashi, 2004). The fossa is present in Saurornitholestes (Rauhut, 2003), but of unreported depth. The fossa is shallow in Ornitholestes (unpublished photo), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002) and Troodon (Varricchio et al., 2002). The fossa is shallow or absent in Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) and Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002). The fossa is absent in Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Segnosaurus (Clark et al., 2004), Microvenator (pers. obs.), Rinchenia (Lu, 2004), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004) and Rahonavis (coding in Forster et al., 1998). A fibula is unknown for Shuvuuia (pers. obs.). The medial fibula is not exposed in any Archaeopteryx specimen.

191. The eleven taxa coded for this are recoded unknown, as it was combined with 190 above.

192. This character is here quantified as an anterior intercondylar groove depth over 20% of the astragalocalcanear transverse width. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Conchoraptor (coding in Lu, 2004), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo) have a shallow intercondylar sulcus on their tibiotarsus.

193. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), Segnosaurus (unpublished photo), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo), Unenlagia (unpublished photos), Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) and Confuciusornis (coding in Norell and Clarke, 2001 and Zhou, 1999) have a single cnemial crest. Oviraptor (Smith, 1992) does not preserve the hindlimb, so cannot be coded. Shuvuuia (pers. obs.), Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998) and Rinchenia (unpublished photo) do not preserve the end of the cnemial crest, so it is uncertain whether there was a medial component.

194. This is a composite character of astragalar ascending process height (0- tall; 1- short; 2- tall), transverse width (0- broad, covering most of anterior surface of distal end of tibia; 1- slender, covering only lateral
half of anterior surface of tibia; 2- restricts it to lateral side of anterior face of distal tibia) and the presence of a medial notch (0- ?; 1-?; 2- with medial notch). Yet the medial notch of parvicursorines only restricts the width of the tip of the process, not the base. Only the height is coded for here, defined as a total astragalocalcanear height greater than 75% of its transverse width. Thus Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) are recoded as having tall processes. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Conchoraptor (coding in Maryanska et al., 2002), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Sinornithosaurus (Xu et al., 1999), Saurornitholestes (Rauhut, 2003) and Rahonavis (Forster et al., 1998) have a tall ascending process.

195. This codes for a "transverse groove or fossa" separating the astragalar condyles from the ascending process, but these are separate characters that can be present in any combination. The presence of a groove matches Norell et al.'s codings best, so is used here. Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975), Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo), Conchoraptor (coding in Lu, 2004) and Velociraptor (Norell and Makovicky, 1999) lack a groove. Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920), Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo), Archaeopteryx (Mayr et al., 2007) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a groove.

196. This is a composite of tibiotarsal fusion and astragalocalcanear fusion. As Norell et al.'s codings match tibiotarsal fusion, only that is used here. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Shuvuuia (pers. obs. of IGM 100/975 and 100/1276), Segnosaurus (unpublished photo of IGM 100/81 and 100/82), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) and Unenlagia (unpublished photo) lack tibiotarsal fusion. Struthiomimus is polymorphic, as Lambe (1917) reports CMN 199 has partial fusion. Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998) and Archaeopteryx (Erickson et al., 2009) are not known from adults, so should be coded unknown.

197. Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979), Conchoraptor (Barsbold et al., 1990), "Ingenia" (Lu, 2004), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) and Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo) have distal tarsals unfused to their metatarsals. Patagonykus (Novas, 1997), Saurornithoides (Norell and Hwang, 2004), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Troodon (Makovicky and Norell, 2004) and Adasaurus (Nesbitt et al., 2011) have distal tarsals fused to their metatarsals. Archaeopteryx is not known from adults (Erickson et al., 2009) so should not be coded.

198. Note no taxon is coded as having state 2- distally fused metatarsals. Chirostenotes (Currie and Russell, 1988), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) do not have fused metatarsals. Erlikosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980) and Dromaeosaurus (Colbert and Russell, 1969) lack distal fusion at least. Oviraptor (Smith, 1992) does not preserve the hindlimb, so cannot be coded.

199. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos), Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004) and Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003) lack a deeply ginglymoid metatarsal II. Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo), Adasaurus (Senter et al., 2004), Utahraptor (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) and Dromaeosaurus (Colbert and Russell, 1969) have a deeply ginglymoid metatarsal II.

200. Caudipteryx (Lu, 2004), Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009), Sinornithosaurus (Xu and Wang, 2000) and Saurornitholestes (unpublished photo) lack a deeply ginglymoid metatarsal III. Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001), Adasaurus (Senter et al., 2004) and Dromaeosaurus (Colbert and Russell, 1969) have a deeply ginglymoid metatarsal III. Troodon is polymorphic, as some specimens (Russell, 1969; Wilson and Currie, 1985) have a deep ginglymus.

201. Sinornithosaurus (White, 2009), Saurornitholestes (unpublished photos), Adasaurus (Senter et al., 2004) and Utahraptor (unpublished info) lack an arctometatarsus. Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) has an arctometatarsus. Sinornithoides lacks a hyperarctometatarsus at least (Currie and Dong, 2001). Avimimus (Vickers-Rich et al., 2002) has a hyperarctometatarsus. The condition in Saurornithoides cannot be determined (pers. obs. of AMNH 6516) since the proximal metatarsus is not preserved.

202. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Erlikosaurus (combination of dorsal view in Barsbold and Perle, 1980 with lateral view in Perle, 1981), Avimimus (Holtz, 1994), Conchoraptor (coding in Lu, 2004), Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009), Sinornithosaurus (White, 2009), Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) Archaeopteryx (combination of dorsal view in Mayr et al., 2007 with lateral view in Wellnhofer, 1974) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) have a metatarsal IV which is not highly flattened dorsoventrally. Ornitholestes (Holtz, 1994), Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979) and Caudipteryx (combination of ventral view in Zhou et al., 2000 with medial view in pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A) have a highly flattened metatarsal IV. Gorgosaurus is polymorphic, as RTMP 94.12.602 (Snively et al., 2004) has a flattened metatarsal IV.

203. This is a composite of metatarsal I proximodistal position (0- in the middle ... of metatarsal II; 1- distal quarter of metatarsal II; 2- near its proximal end; 3- NA), anteroposterior position (0- medial surface; 1- posterior surface; 2- medial surface; 3- NA) and presence (0- present; 1- present; 2- present; 3- absent). As Middleton (2003) established, even Aves do not attach metatarsal I to the posterior surface of the metatarsus, instead the metatarsal is twisted to allow the digit to be reversed. Thus only the proximodistal placement is coded here, as a quantified ordered character- metatarsal I ends >30% up shaft of metatarsal II (0); 18-30% (1); <18% (2). Erlikosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980) and Chirostenotes (Glut, 1997) have a proximally placed metatarsal I. Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) has an intermediate position for metatarsal I. Caudipteryx (Zhou et al., 2000), Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) and Troodon (Sternberg, 1932) have distally placed first metatarsals. Taxa without first metatarsals are recoded inapplicable- Gallimimus (Osmolska et al., 1972), Struthiomimus (Osborn, 1916) and Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987). As Norell and Makovicky (1997) determined the ridge/scar on the posteromedial edge of metatarsal II is unrelated to the first metatarsal placement, taxa with disarticulated pes like Sinraptor (Currie and Zhao, 1994), Allosaurus (Gilmore, 1920 articulated it incorrectly; Chure, 2000), Tyrannosaurus (Osborn, 1906; Brochu, 2003), Shuvuuia (pers. obs.), Mononykus (Perle et al., 1994), Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994), IGM 100/42 (unpublished photo shows it is not articulated), "Ingenia" (unpublished photo shows it articulated incorrectly) and Deinonychus (Ostrom, 1969 articulated it incorrectly) are coded unknown.

204. Saurornithoides (pers. obs. of AMNH 6516), Troodon (Sternberg, 1932) and Adasaurus (coding in Senter, 2007) lack tarsal contact with metatarsal I. Erlikosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980) has contact. Avimimus (Kurzanov, 1987) is inapplicable as it lacks metatarsal I. Described Shuvuuia specimens (pers. obs.) do not preserve metatarsal I. The proximal portion of Alxasaurus' metatarsal I is unknown (Russell and Dong, 1994).

205. This is a composite character involving pedal phalanx II-2 being "highly modified for extreme hyperextension" (itself involving multiple variables), pedal ungual II curvature and size. As in Senter (2007), only the latter is coded for here, quantified as >140% of pedal ungual III length. Ornitholestes (unpublished photos; based on ungual height), Caudipteryx (pers. obs. of NGMC 97-9-A), Rinchenia (unpublished photo; based on comparison to ungual IV) and Adasaurus (Barsbold, 1983; coding in Senter, 2007) have a small pedal ungual II. Saurornitholestes (unpublished photos) and Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) have a large pedal ungual II. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Garudimimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005), Shuvuuia (pers. obs.), Byronosaurus (Makovicky et al., 2003), Achillobator (Senter, 2007) and Dromaeosaurus (Colbert and Russell, 1969) do not preserve ungual II. Saurornithoides does not preserve unguals III or IV (Norell et al., 2009) and ungual II is not large enough to be certain it was in the enlarged range.

 

Xu et al., 2002 on Sinovenator

The consensus tree shown is correct, but there are 172,339 mpts of 576 steps, not 336 mpts as reported by Xu et al..

7. As redefined above, Sinovenator lacks a subotic recess, so must be coded inapplicable for the presence of an enlarged lateral depression.

15. Sinovenator's posterior tympanic recess is within its otic recess (Xu et al., 2002).

22. Sinovenator lacks a ventral rim on its antorbital fossa (Senter et al., 2004).

24. Sinovenator has a short dorsal jugal process (unpublished photo).

32. Sinovenator at least has a short anterodorsal lacrimal process (based on reconstruction of skull using known elements).

34. Sinovenator has a wedge-shaped nasofrontal suture (coding in Senter, 2007).

58. Sinovenator lacks a medial/dorsal articular process (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

63. Sinovenator lacks serrated teeth (Senter et al., 2004).

65. Sinovenator must be coded inapplicable, as it lacks serrations.

66. Sinovenator must be coded inapplicable, as it lacks serrations.

67. Sinovenator has constricted tooth roots (coding in Senter et al., 2004).

70. Sinovenator has lingually flat premaxillary teeth (coding in Xu et al., 2004).

78. Sinovenator is provisionally coded unknown for cervical neural spine length until it can be verified.

85. Sinovenator is provisionally coded unknown for anterior dorsal transverse process length until it can be verified.

87. Sinovenator has apically extensive interspinous ligament grooves on its dorsal vertebrae (coding in Senter, 2007).

113. Sinovenator has a 45 degree angle between its proximal and distal deltopectoral crest edges (unpublished photo).

114. Sinovenator lacks a lateral ridge on its deltopectoral crest (unpublished photo).

115. Sinovenator lacks a hypertrophied olecranon (unpublished photo).

125. Sinovenator lacks an enlarged manual ungual I (unpublished photo).

126. Sinovenator lacks a proximodorsal lip on its manual unguals (unpublished photos).

127. Sinovenator has a large ventral hook on its preacetabular process (Xu et al., 2002).

129. Sinovenator lacks divergent postacetabular processes (Xu et al., 2002).

132. Sinovenator's cuppedicus fossa extends posteriorly close to the acetabulum (Xu et al., 2002).

133. Sinovenator's cuppedicus fossa has a lateral overhang (Xu et al., 2002).

135. Sinovenator has such a reduced ischial shaft that it is coded inapplicable for curvature (Xu et al., 2002).

138. This character differs from Norell et al.'s original character 170 in lacking the state for an obturator foramen which no coded taxon has. Sinovenator has an obturator notch (Xu et al., 2002).

143. Sinovenator is mesopubic (Xu et al., 2002).

159. There does not seem to be much of a medial cnemial crest in Sinovenator (Xu et al., 2002).

161. As this character codes for a groove or fossa separating the astragalar ascending process from the condyles, and it was noted above that the features vary independently, Sinovenator is recoded unknown here until its morphology is described.

203. Metatarsal IV in Sinovenator is not highly flattened (White, 2009).

205. Sinovenator lacks a supratrochanteric process (Xu et al., 2002).

206. This character is here defined as a metatarsal IV which is >166% the transverse width of metatarsal II at midshaft. Oddly every taxon not coded 0 or 1 is coded inapplicable, except Byronosaurus and Zanabazar. Erlikosaurus (Barsbold and Perle, 1980), Sinovenator (White, 2009) and Sinornithoides (Currie and Dong, 2001) have subequal metatarsals II and IV. Conchoraptor (Barsbold et al., 1990) and Zanabazar (Norell et al., 2009) have a robust metatarsal IV compared to metatarsal II. The condition in Alxasaurus is unknown, as the two preserved proximal metatarsals are either III and IV or II and III, so only the distal end of metatarsal II can be compared to metatarsal IV (Russell and Dong, 1994).

 

Hwang et al., 2002 on Microraptor

Taxon issues-

Microraptor zhaoianus- This includes the holotype, two specimens described by Hwang et al. (2002), the M. gui holotype, two specimens described as Cryptovolans, NGMC 00-12-A described as Sinornithosaurus by Ji (2002) and a few undescribed specimens (see Microraptor entry).

1. Microraptor has asymmetrical remiges (Xu et al., 2003).

2. Microraptor has a round orbit (Ji, 2002).

3. Microraptor lacks an orbital postorbital process (unpublished photo of IVPP V13352).

4. Microraptor lacks a posteriorly convex postorbital bar (unpublished photo of IVPP V13352).

13. Microraptor has a dorsal tympanic recess (Turner et al., 2011).

17. Microraptor has a V-shaped premaxillary symphysis (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

20. Microraptor has a maxillary fenestra (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

21. Microraptor has a promaxillary fenestra (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

22. Microraptor has a strong rim on its antorbital fossa (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

23. Microraptor lacks a highly pneumatic nasal (Ji, 2002; Xu et al., 2000).

25. Microraptor has a strap-like posterior jugal process (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

30. Microraptor lacks a dorsal lacrimal horn (Ji, 2002). While Xu and Zhang (2005) code it as lacking a lateral one as well, it is provisionally retained as 0/2 here, since they follow Norell et al. in miscoding other dromaeosaurids.

31. Microraptor lacks a pneumatic lacrimal (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

32. Using the redefined states above, Microraptor is coded 1 as it has an elongate posterodorsal lacrimal process (Ji, 2002) and Xu and Zhang (2005) code it as lacking a long anterodorsal process.

33. Microraptor lacks a prefrontal (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

35. Microraptor has a parietal sagittal crest (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

36. Microraptor has fused parietals (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

40. Microraptor has a strongly inclined quadrate (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

37. Microraptor has a ventrally projected ventral jugal process (unpublished photo of IVPP V13352).

50. Microraptor lacks a medially curved dentary symphysis (Xu et al., 2000).

51. Microraptor lacks a prominent coronoid process (Ji, 2002).

52. Microraptor has a posterodorsal dentary process (Hwang et al., 2002), but its length compared to the mandibular fenestra is unknown.

54. Microraptor lacks a surangular process invading the external mandibular fenestra (Ji, 2002).

58. Microraptor has a dorsal articular process (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

59. Microraptor has a short retroarticular process (Xu et al., 2000).

60. Microraptor lacks a caenagnathoid-like elongate sliding mandibular joint (Xu et al., 2000).

63. Hwang et al. divide Norell et al.'s (2001) original character 85 into two- one for maxillary teeth and one for dentary teeth. Why this is done is uncertain, as every taxon is coded the same for each even when one of the bones is unknown. Furthermore, no described taxon definitely varies in this character between maxillary and dentary teeth. Troodon is a possible exception, as Currie (1987) described maxillary teeth as having mesial serrations while dentary teeth sometimes lacked them. Yet no maxilla has been described with teeth, making Currie's identifications based on size alone, and it's possible some of the smaller teeth are posterior maxillary teeth instead. Still, for now a coding of 0 may be best for character 63. Ornitholestes and Adasaurus should be coded 0/1, as their teeth are serrated but the extent has not been described. Saurornitholestes has at least three anterior maxillary teeth with both carinae serrated (Currie and Varricchio, 2004), but whether any of the many isolated Saurornitholestes teeth without mesial serrations are maxillary is unknown. It it best coded 0/1. Similarly, Velociraptor has at least some maxillary teeth with both carinae serrated (Barsbold and Osmolska, 1999), and whether any isolated teeth lacking mesial serrations are maxillary is unknown. Sues (1977) did not mention any teeth lacking mesial serrations. Deinonychus has anterior serrations in all described teeth (Ostrom, 1969; Brinkman et al., 1998). Sinovenator lacks serrations (Senter et al., 2004). Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack dentary teeth, so should be coded inapplicable. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) does not preserve definite maxillary teeth, so cannot be coded. Mononykus only preserves one tooth (Perle et al., 1993) of uncertain position, so cannot be coded. Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979) does not preserve maxillary teeth.

64. Ornitholestes and Adasaurus should be coded 0/1, as their teeth are serrated but the extent has not been described. Saurornithoides (Norell et al., 2009) seems to lack mesial serrations on at least some teeth, but posterior serrations are not exposed, so should be coded 1/2. Sues (1977) did not mention any teeth in Velociraptor lacking mesial serrations, and whether any isolated teeth lacking mesial serrations are maxillary is unknown. Deinonychus has anterior serrations in all described teeth (Ostrom, 1969; Brinkman et al., 1998). Sinovenator lacks serrations (Senter et al., 2004). Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack dentary teeth, so should be coded inapplicable. Mononykus only preserves one tooth (Perle et al., 1993) of uncertain position, so cannot be coded. Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) does not preserve dentary teeth.

68. Hwang et al. divide Norell et al.'s (2001) original character 89 into two- one for maxillary teeth and one for dentary teeth. As with 63/64 above, every taxon is coded the same for each even when one of the bones is unknown (except Allosaurus and "Ingenia" are coded unknown for the dentary tooth character this time). Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) lacks constricted bases on its teeth. Sinovenator has constricted tooth roots (coding in Senter et al., 2004). Only some maxillary teeth have constricted roots in Archaeopteryx (Howgate, 1984), so it should be coded polymorphic. Harpymimus (Kobayashi and Barsbold, 2005) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack maxillary teeth, so should be coded inapplicable. Mononykus only preserves one tooth (Perle et al., 1993) of uncertain position, so cannot be coded. Alxasaurus (Russell and Dong, 1994) does not preserve definite maxillary teeth, so cannot be coded. Segnosaurus (Perle, 1979) and Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) do not preserve maxillary teeth.

69. Allosaurus lacks basally constricted dentary teeth. Adasaurus (coding in Longrich and Currie, 2009) lacks constricted bases on its teeth. Sinovenator has constricted tooth roots (coding in Senter et al., 2004). Only some dentary teeth have constricted roots in Erlikosaurus (Clark et al., 2004), Microraptor (Xu et al., 2000) and Archaeopteryx (Howgate, 1984; Elzanowski and Wellnhofer, 1996), so it should be coded polymorphic. Microvenator (Makovicky and Sues, 1998),"Ingenia" (Barsbold et al., 1990) and Confuciusornis (Chiappe et al., 1999) lack dentary teeth, so should be coded inapplicable. Mononykus only preserves one tooth (Perle et al., 1993) of uncertain position, so cannot be coded. Utahraptor (Kirkland et al., 1993) and Achillobator (Perle et al., 1999) do not preserve dentary teeth.

73. Microraptor has ten cervicals (Xu et al., 2003).

77. Only posterior cervicals were known from Microraptor when Hwang et al. coded this character, and more recently discovered specimens do not show it clearly, so the taxon should be coded unknown for posterior centrum extent.

80. As only a posterior cervical neural spine is well exposed in Microraptor (Hwang et al., 2002), it is coded unknown for this neural spine length given the more strict definition above which covers cervicals 3-5.

82. Microraptor lacks opisthocoelous cervical vertebrae (Hwang et al., 2002).

85. Microraptor has dorsal hyposphene-hypantrum articulations (Hwang et al., 2002).

87. As dorsal vertebrae 1-3 of Microraptor specimens described by Hwang et al. do not preserve transverse processes (dorsal 4 may, but is unfigured), it cannot be coded for the character as redefined above.

91. Microraptor has ventrally flat or grooved posterior sacral centra (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

92. Microraptor lacks sacral pleurocoels (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).

93. Microraptor lacks a procoelous last sacral (coding in Xu and Zhang, 2005).
